Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Obligatory and Recommended Bucket Numbers for Well Purification
States obligatory and recommended bucket numbers with proof from athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory and recommended bucket numbers for purifying a well after the death of a pigeon/chicken and a mouse/sparrow, and explain why these numbers are accepted despite contradicting qiyas.

Core Idea
Pigeon deaths require 40 obligatory buckets (up to 60 recommended); mouse deaths require 20 obligatory (up to 30) — based on athar, not qiyas.

This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on well purification when certain animals die in it. It specifies exact bucket numbers for larger birds and small animals, distinguishes between obligatory and recommended amounts, and defends these numbers against the criticism that they contradict analogical reasoning. The source of the numbers is athar (reports) from the Companions and Followers, not pure qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animal categories and their rulings
The text distinguishes two categories: larger birds (like pigeon or chicken) and small animals (like mouse or sparrow). For each, it gives an obligatory number and a recommended maximum.
The obligatory number is wajib; the recommended upper bound is mustahabb.
2
State the obligatory and recommended numbers
For larger birds: obligatory 40 buckets, recommended up to 60. For small animals: obligatory 20 buckets, recommended up to 30. These numbers apply when the animal dies in the well and, for larger birds, has not swollen.
The text notes that swelling changes the ruling for larger birds; if swollen, different numbers may apply (not detailed here).
3
Recognize the evidence (dalil)
The numbers are not derived from qiyas but from athar (reports) from Companions like Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr, and from Followers like al-Sha'bi, al-Nakha'i, and Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman. Some marfu' reports exist but are not considered authentic.
The Hanafi school follows these athar as established practice.
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani estimated 40 or 50 buckets for larger animals, while others (like al-Quduri) said 40 to 60. The text notes that al-Bahr al-Ra'iq considers Muhammad's view easier but weaker. The relied-upon fatwa is the range 40-60 and 20-30.
The delegation to two men (from Nasir ibn Salam) is mentioned as a cautious approach.
5
Address the qiyas objection
An objector might say that analogy requires either drawing all water if impure or none if pure. The response is that these specific numbers come from revealed sources (athar), which take precedence over qiyas.
The Hanafi method is to rely on transmitted reports in such cases.

Source Text

The Book of Purity / Purifications, Wells, and Traces. And regarding something like a pigeon or a chicken that dies in it (the well), forty to sixty (buckets). The statement of al-Nasafi in al-Kanz, and in Khizanat al-Muftiyin: 'Upon it is the fatwa.' And he said in al-Bahr al-Ra'iq: 'The verification differed in the issue, and giving fatwa with what is from Muhammad is easier, and acting upon what is from Nasir ibn Salam (may Allah have mercy on him) of delegation to two men is more cautious; therefore he said in al-Mukhtar: What is from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) is facility, but its weakness is not hidden, for if the legal ruling is to draw all the water due to the ruling of impurity, then the statement of restricting to drawing a specific number of buckets depends on a revealed evidence that indicates it, and where is that? Rather the reported from Ibn Abbas and Ibn al-Zubayr contradicts it.' End quote. And in al-Hidayah and others: 'Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) only estimated this because he observed in his town that water in wells is this amount usually.' And regarding something like a mouse or a sparrow, twenty to thirty. So regarding a pigeon and a chicken and what approximates them, if it dies in the well and has not swollen, one draws as obligatory forty buckets, as al-Tahawi extracted from al-Sha'bi, al-Nakha'i, and Hammad ibn Abi Sulayman. As for the amount of recommendation, the author - following al-Quduri - mentioned up to sixty, and it is not based on a source; for what is reported in the athar from the Companions and the Followers, as we have detailed in al-Si'aya, in such a case is either forty, fifty, or seventy. And Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir in such a case forty or fifty, and the author of al-Khulasa and al-Muhit chose it. And regarding a mouse or a sparrow, twenty is drawn as obligatory, and as recommendation up to thirty. For if you say: These estimates in such cases for purifying the well contradict analogy, for analogy requires that if the well becomes impure by the death of animals, it is obligatory to draw all the water in every case, and if it does not become impure, nothing must be drawn at all. So from where do these estimates come? I say: There have come athar in this chapter from the Companions and the Followers, and some of them mentioned marfu' reports as well but they were not established; so they followed those athar and said: The issues of this chapter are based on them, and they annexed to the transmitted cases their equivalents by way of the indication of the text, and in the position...

Commentary
This passage is a legal commentary on the ruling for purifying a well when an animal dies in it. The author discusses two categories: larger birds like pigeons and chickens require an obligatory drawing of 40 buckets of water, with a recommended amount up to 60. Smaller animals like mice and sparrows require an obligatory 20 buckets, with recommendation up to 30. The passage notes a difference of opinion: some scholars, including Muhammad al-Shaybani, set the numbers at 40 or 50, while others report 40, 50, or 70 from the Companions. The commentary then addresses a potential objection: these estimates appear to contradict qiyas (analogical reasoning), which would require either removing all water if impurity is confirmed or none if not. The response is that these rulings are based on athar (narrations) from the Companions and Followers, not on pure analogy. The Hanafi school accepts these estimates as established by practice, and later scholars annexed similar cases by textual indication. The mention of al-Nasafi and al-Bahr al-Ra'iq indicates that the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is the estimation as given, though there is some preference for Muhammad's view due to ease.

Key Terms
نزح (nazh): Drawing water from a well, specifically the number of buckets required for purification.
حمامة (hamāmah): A pigeon or dove; used here as a benchmark for larger birds.
فارة (fārah): A mouse or rat; used as a benchmark for small animals.
دجاجة (dajājah): A chicken; included in the larger bird category for well purification.
عصفور (usfūr): A sparrow; included in the small animal category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school specifies wajib bucket numbers (40/20) for well purification based on athar, not qiyas, with intra-madhhab variations and a mufta bihi range.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
6 min
Rulings on Purification of Well and Standard Bucket Size
Rulings on quantities of water to draw and timing of impurity in a well

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the obligatory and recommended numbers of buckets to draw from a well after animal contamination, identify the standard bucket measure per Zahir al-Riwayah, and apply the timing rules for impurity retroactivity.

Core Idea
Well purification amounts are based on transmitted precedents from the Companions, not analogy.

This lesson covers the Hanafi rulings on purifying a well when an animal dies in it. The numbers of buckets to draw—20 for small animals, 40 for larger ones—are established by athar from the Companions, not by qiyas. We will also examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the standard bucket is the well's own bucket or a general middle bucket, and the rules for determining when the well became impure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling for each animal type
Locate in the text the obligatory numbers for a mouse/bird (20 buckets) and for a pigeon/hen (40 buckets). Note that additional numbers up to 30 or 60 are recommended (mustahabb).
The passage also mentions that the 60-figure has no solid basis.
2
Trace the dalil for the numbers
The text cites athar from al-Sha'bi, al-Nakha'i, Hammad, and others. Recognize that these are transmitted precedents (qawl sahabi), not marfu' hadith.
The Hanafi school relies on these as authoritative in matters where qiyas is not applied.
3
Understand the response to the qiyas objection
The author anticipates an objection: these estimates contradict qiyas, which would require drawing all water if impure or none if pure. The response is that these rulings are based on athar, not qiyas.
This illustrates the Hanafi methodology of giving precedence to transmitted precedents over analogy in this domain.
4
Recognize the intra-Hanafi dispute on the standard bucket
Two positions exist: the well's own bucket (Zahir al-Riwayah, chosen by al-Hidayah, al-Muhit, al-Bada’i') and the middle bucket (chosen by al-Kanz, al-Fiqh al-Nafi', Multaqa al-Abhar). The relied-upon view is the well's own bucket.
The practical consequence: drawing with a larger bucket still counts as one bucket if the well's own bucket is the standard.
5
Apply the timing rules for impurity retroactivity
If the time of the animal's fall is known, the well becomes impure from that moment. If unknown, it is considered impure from one day and night if not swollen, and from three days and nights if swollen.
This rule is based on the physical state of the carcass.

'al-wasat' with fatha on both letters, or with fatha on the first and sukun on the second, meaning the medium between the small and the large. This is according to what the author and the author of 'al-Kanz', 'al-Fiqh al-Nafi'', 'Multaqa al-Abhar', and others chose. And the author of 'al-Hidaya', 'al-Muhit', 'al-Bada’i', and others chose what is the Zahir al-Riwayah, which is considering the bucket of that well. And the truth is what is in 'Jam' al-Mudmarat' and others, that the standard is the bucket of that well in which the impurity fell, and if it has no specific bucket or it was not drawn with it, then the standard is the middle bucket. [2] He said: 'And what exceeds it' means the middle bucket; so if one draws the obligatory amount with one large bucket, that suffices, and it is the apparent [position] of the madhhab that the purpose is achieved.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining the purification of a well when an animal dies in it. The text first gives the quantities: for a mouse or bird, 20 buckets are obligatory and up to 30 are recommended; for a pigeon, hen, or similar, if it dies without swelling, 40 buckets are obligatory. The author cites a narration from al-Tahawi to support the 40-bucket figure. Concerning the recommended amount, the author notes that the figure of 60 buckets mentioned by al-Quduri has no solid basis; the actual transmitted reports from the Companions and Successors mention 40, 50, or 70 buckets. Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir mentioned 40 or 50, and this was preferred by the authors of al-Khulasa and al-Muhit. The commentary then addresses a hypothetical objection: these estimates contradict qiyas (analogy), which would require either drawing all water if the well becomes impure or none if it does not. The answer is that the Hanafi school bases these rulings on transmitted traditions (athar) from the Companions and Successors, not on qiyas. The jurists then analogized similar cases by way of dalalat al-nass (textual indication). Next, the passage defines the standard bucket (al-dalu al-wasat). There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars, including the author of al-Hidaya and others, adopt the position of Zahir al-Riwayah that the standard is the actual bucket of that well. Others, including the author of al-Kanz, adopt the middle bucket. The correct view according to Jam' al-Mudmarat is that the well's own bucket is used; if there is no specific bucket or it is not used, then the middle bucket. The phrase 'wa ma jawazahu ihtusiba bih' means that if a larger bucket is used, it counts toward the required number. Finally, the passage rules on when the well becomes impure: from the moment the animal falls in if known, otherwise from one day and night if not swollen, and from three days and nights if swollen.

Key Terms
دلو وسط (dalu wasat): The middle-sized bucket, between a small and a large bucket, used as a standard measure for drawing water from a well for purification.
نزح (nazh): Drawing or removing water from a well, especially for purification purposes after it has been contaminated.
انتفخ (intafakha): To swell, referring to the condition of a dead animal found in a well; if swollen, it indicates the impurity has spread, affecting the time from which the well is considered impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on well purification numbers is based on transmitted precedents from the Salaf, not on qiyas, and the standard bucket is the well's own bucket per Zahir al-Riwayah.

Taharah — Purification

The Standard Bucket for Well Purification
Intra-Hanafi dispute over the standard bucket for well purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preferred Hanafi position on the standard bucket for well purification and explain the intra-madhhab dispute.

Core Idea
The standard bucket is the well's own bucket; only if absent does the middle bucket apply.

Determining the correct bucket for measuring water removal from a well is essential for proper purification. The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement: should the standard be the actual bucket used for that well, or the 'middle bucket' (al-dalw al-wasat)? This passage presents both views and the preferred ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Find the view that the standard is the middle bucket and the view that it is the bucket of that well.
The first position is attributed to the author of this text and others; the second is the Zahir al-Riwayah position.
2
Note the supporting scholars
List which scholars or works support each position: for middle bucket (al-Kanz, al-Fiqh al-Nafi', Multaqa al-Abhar); for well's bucket (al-Hidayah, al-Muhit, al-Bada'i').
This helps understand the weight of each view.
3
Determine the preferred view
From Jami' al-Mudmarat, the preferred view (al-haqq) is the well's bucket, and the middle bucket is used only if there is no specific bucket or it was not used.
This is the mufta bihi ruling.
4
Apply the ruling
If a well has a known bucket, use it to measure the required number of bucketfuls. If not, use a middle-sized bucket. Also, a larger bucket counts proportionally.
For example, if one large bucket equals two middle buckets, it counts as two.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الدلو - بفتح الدال وسكون اللام -.
الوسط - بفتحتين، وفتح الأوّل وسكون الثاني – في المتوسط بين الصغير والكبير. هذا على ما اختاره المصنف له ومؤلف «الكنز»، و«الفقه النافع»، و«ملتقى الأبحر»، وغيرها.
واختار صاحب «الهداية»، و«المحيط»، و«البدائع»، وغيرهم ما هو ظاهر الرواية من اعتبار دلو تلك البئر. والحق ما في «جامع المضمرات» وغيره أنَّ المعتبر هو دلو تلك البئر التي وقعت النجاسة فيها، فإن لم يكن لها دلو معين أو لم ينزح به فالمعتبر الدلو الوسط.
[٢] قوله : وما جاوزه ؛ أي الدلو الوسط ، فلو نزح قدر الواجب بدلو واحد كبير كفى ذلك ، وهو ظاهر المذهب الحصول المقصود.
And the standard is the middle bucket, and whatever exceeds it is counted. He said: 'And the standard' means in drawing water. 'Al-dalw' with fatha on dal and sukun on lam. 'Al-wasat' with two fatha or fatha on first and sukun on second, meaning the medium between small and large. This is according to what the author chose and the author of al-Kanz, al-Fiqh al-Nafi', Multaqa al-Abhar, and others. And the author of al-Hidayah, al-Muhit, al-Bada'i', and others chose what is apparent from the riwaya: consideration of the bucket of that well. And the truth is in Jami' al-Mudmarat and others that the standard is the bucket of that well in which the impurity fell, and if it has no specific bucket or it was not drawn with it, then the standard is the middle bucket. [2] He said: 'And whatever exceeds it' means the middle bucket, so if one drew the required amount with one large bucket, that suffices, and that is the apparent madhhab for achieving the intended purpose.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the standard bucket (al-dalw al-wasat) used for measuring water removal from a well during purification. The base ruling states that the standard is the middle bucket, and if a larger bucket is used, it is counted proportionally (i.e., one large bucketful may count as more than one standard bucketful). The commentary explains that some scholars, like the author of al-Hidayah, al-Muhit, and al-Bada'i', follow the Zahir al-Riwayah position that the standard is the actual bucket of that well (i.e., the bucket commonly used for that well). Others, like the author of Jami' al-Mudmarat, hold that the standard is the middle bucket only if there is no specific bucket for that well or if it was not used. The preferred view (al-haqq) is that the standard is the bucket of that well, and if there is no specific bucket, then the middle bucket is used. The second part clarifies that if one draws the required amount of water with a single large bucket (larger than the standard), it is sufficient because the excess is counted as multiple standard buckets.

Key Terms
الدلو الوسط (al-dalw al-wasat): The middle-sized bucket used for drawing water from a well, considered the standard for measuring the quantity of water to be removed for purification.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the preferred view (al-haqq) regarding the standard bucket for well purification?
A) The middle bucket is always the standard.
B) The bucket of the well is the standard if available; otherwise the middle bucket.
C) The largest bucket is always the standard.
D) The standard is determined by the depth of the well.

Key Takeaway
The standard bucket for well purification is the well's own bucket; the middle bucket is only used when no specific bucket exists.

Timing of Well Contamination: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on well impurity timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the time from which a well is considered impure after finding a dead animal, and apply the principle of al-yaqin la yazulu bil-shakk.

Core Idea
When the time of contamination is unknown, the Sahibayn rule from discovery; Abu Hanifa estimates using swelling.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the timing of impurity (najasa) in a well when a dead animal is found. The ruling differs based on whether the time of the animal's fall is known. The two principles at play are certainty versus estimation based on apparent signs. Understanding this dispute helps clarify how Hanafi jurists balance legal presumption (istishab) and circumstantial evidence (qara'in).

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
If the time of the animal's fall is known, the well is impure from that time. If unknown, an estimation must be made.
2
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that impurity begins from the moment of discovery (mawjud). This is based on qiyas: certainty is not removed by doubt (al-yaqin la yazulu bil-shakk).
3
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa distinguishes by swelling: if the carcass is swollen, impurity dates from three days prior; if not swollen, from one day prior. Swelling indicates old age and typical decomposition time.
4
Analyze the proofs
Sahibayn rely on the principle that certainty cannot be overridden by doubt, so the well is assumed pure until the moment of discovery. Abu Hanifa relies on attributing to the apparent cause (sabab zahir) when causes are hidden; the swelling is a visible sign that can be used for estimation.
5
Identify the practical difference and fatwa
The difference affects when one must repeat prayers and wash items. The fatwa is generally on the Sahibayn's view, but al-Sabbaghi differentiated: Abu Hanifa's view for prayer-related matters, Sahibayn's view for other matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويتنجس البئر من وقتِ الوقوع إن عُلِمَ ذلك، وإلا فمنذ يوم وليلة إن لم ينتفخ، ومنذ ثلاثة أيام ولياليها إن انتفخ
قوله : ويتنجس؛ أي يحكم بنجاسة البئر، فلو توضأ منه أو اغتسل مع علم وقت الوقوع يعيد الصلوات، ويغسل ما غسل منه، هذا إذا علم؛ أي يقيناً أو ظنّاً وقت الوقوع، وإن لم يعلم ذلك يحكم بنجاسته من وقت الوجود والاطلاع عليه عندهما مطلقا، وهو القياس؛ لأنّ اليقين لا يزولُ بالشك. والأصلُ إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته، وذلك لأنا تيقنا بطهارته فيما سبق، ووقع الشك في نجاسته بعد ذلك، فلا يحكم به؛ لاحتمال أن يكون مات في غير البئر، ثم ألقتها الريح العاصف أو بعض السفهاء فيها. وعند أبي حنيفة لله لو وجد منتفخاً أو متفسخاً يحكم بنجاستِهِ من ابتداء ثلاثةِ أيام ولياليها؛ لأنّ الإحالة على السبب الظاهر واجب عند خفاء الأسباب، والكون في الماء سبب ظاهر فيحمل عليه، والانتفاخُ دليل التقادم فيقدر بالثلاث؛ لأنه يحصل في هذه المدة غالباً، وأما لو وجد غير منتفخ فيقدر عنده بيوم وليلة؛ لأن ما دون ذلك ساعات غير منضبطة، وفي المقام أبحاث مبسوطة في شروح «الهداية».
وقالا : مذ وجد.
قوله : وقالا مذ وجد؛ أي ذلك النجس في البئر، قال في «الجوهرة النيرة شرح مختصر القدوري»: «عليه الفتوى». انتهى. وفي «المجتبى»: «كان ركنُ الأئمة الصباغي يفتى بقول أبي حنيفة له فيما يتعلق بالصلاة، وبقولهما فيما سواه، يعني في غسل الثوب والبدن والأواني وغير ذلك مما وصل إليه ذلك الماء».
And the well becomes impure from the time of fall if that is known, otherwise from a day and night if it is not swollen, and from three days and nights if it is swollen. He said: 'And it becomes impure' means the well is ruled impure. So if one performed wudu or ghusl with knowledge of the time of fall, he must repeat the prayers and wash what he washed. This if he knows (certainly or by strong presumption) the time of fall. But if he does not know, then in the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) impurity is ruled from the time of discovery and sighting absolutely, and that is the qiyas because certainty is not removed by doubt. The principle is to attribute the incident to the nearest possible time, because we are certain of its purity before, and doubt about impurity after, so we do not rule it due to the possibility that the animal died outside the well and then a storm wind or some foolish person threw it in. According to Abu Hanifa, if it is found swollen or disintegrated, he rules impurity from the beginning of three days and nights, because attributing to the apparent cause is obligatory when causes are hidden, and being in water is an apparent cause so it is attributed; swelling indicates oldness and is estimated by three because it usually happens in that period. But if found not swollen, he estimates from a day and night because less than that is unregulated hours. And there are extensive researches in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. And the two said: from when found. He said: 'And the two said from when found' meaning that impurity in the well. He said in al-Jawharat al-Nayyirah sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: 'The fatwa is upon it.' End. And in al-Mujtaba: 'Rukn al-A'immah al-Sabbaghi used to give fatwa according to Abu Hanifa regarding matters of prayer, and according to the two regarding other matters, i.e., washing clothes, body, vessels, etc. that came into contact with that water.'
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the time from which a well is considered impure when a dead animal is found in it. The base ruling states: if the time of the animal's fall is known, impurity dates from that time; if unknown, it is estimated: one day and night if not swollen, three days and nights if swollen. The commentary explains that the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that impurity begins from the moment of discovery (mawjud) because certainty of past purity cannot be overridden by doubt (al-yaqin la yazulu bil-shakk). Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on swelling: if swollen, impurity begins three days prior; if not, one day prior, because swelling indicates old age and the estimate is based on typical decomposition time. The divergent views lead to practical differences in when to repeat prayers and wash items. The final note indicates that the fatwa is on the view of the two (Sahibayn) according to al-Jawharat al-Nayyirah, but some scholars like al-Sabbaghi differentiated: Abu Hanifa's view is followed for prayer-related matters, the two's view for other matters.

Key Terms
منذ يوم وليلة (mundhu yawm wa laylah): An estimation of time: one full day (24 hours) prior to discovery, used when the carcass is not swollen.
منذ ثلاثة أيام ولياليها (mundhu thalathat ayyam wa layaliha): An estimation of three full days and nights prior to discovery, used when the carcass is swollen.
انتفخ (intafakha): The carcass is swollen, indicating it has been in the water for some time (usually three days).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the basis for Abu Hanifa's estimation of three days when a swollen carcass is found?
A) Because swelling usually occurs within three days in water.
B) Because three days is the maximum time for impurity.
C) Because the Sahibayn agreed on three days.
D) Because the well becomes impure only after three days.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when the time of fall is unknown, the Sahibayn rule impurity from discovery, while Abu Hanifa uses swelling to estimate prior contamination; the mufta bihi is the Sahibayn's view, with an exception for prayer.

Definition of Su'r and Its Scope in Hanafi Fiqh
Defining the term su'r and its expanded usage in Hanafi fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the term su'r (سؤر) in Hanafi fiqh and explain its scope of application to both liquids and solids.

Core Idea
Su'r is the leftover after drinking – its ruling extends to all food remnants.

In this passage, the commentator explains the term 'su'r' that appears in the matn. He clarifies that after discussing rulings on wells, the author addresses su'r because some well rulings depend on remnants. Su'r literally means the leftover in a vessel after drinking, but its usage became general for food too. Understanding this definition is essential for subsequent purity rules regarding leftovers of humans and animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its orthography
Note that su'r is spelled with a dammah on the 'seen' and a hamzah on the 'ayn. This linguistic precision is important for correct pronunciation and transmission.
The scholars emphasize the exact wording to avoid confusion with similar terms.
2
State the literal meaning
The original meaning is 'the leftover after drinking that remains in the vessel' – specifically the liquid left after consumption.
The word derives from the concept of remaining quantity.
3
Recognize the expansion of usage
The passage states that su'r's usage became general ('umma) to include food and other things. This means the term now applies to any remnant left after consumption, not just drink.
This expansion is key for applying purity rules to diverse leftovers.
4
Understand the placement in the text
Su'r is discussed after wells because some rulings on wells depend on rulings on remnants (aathar). The arrangement shows the logical interdependence of fiqh topics.
This contextual placement helps in memorizing and connecting related rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And su'r. He said: 'And su'r...etc.' Because some issues of wells depend on issues of remains, he mentioned the rulings of su'r after the rulings of the well. It is with dammah on the seen, hamzated 'ayn: a name for the leftover after drink that the drinker leaves in the vessel, then its usage became general for food and other things.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is a definition ruling for the term 'su'r' (remnant). The commentator explains that after discussing wells, the author turns to su'r because well rulings sometimes depend on rulings about remnants (aathar). The term su'r (with dammah on sin and hamzah on 'ayn) literally means the leftover liquid in a vessel after someone drinks from it. Over time, its usage expanded to include leftovers of food and other substances. This definition is foundational for later rulings on the purity of leftovers from humans and animals.

Key Terms
سؤر (su'r): The leftover liquid (or food) in a vessel after someone has consumed from it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Su'r is the leftover after consumption, originally for drinks but later expanded to food; its definition is crucial for taharah rulings on leftovers.

The Dispute on Timing of Well Water Impurity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on timing of water impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the timing of water impurity when a dead animal is found in a well, and explain the proof (qiyas vs istihsan) for each position.

Core Idea
Two Hanafi views: impurity from discovery (Sahibayn) vs. estimated times based on condition (Abu Hanifa).

This passage introduces a well-known intra-Hanafi dispute on purity jurisprudence. When a dead animal is found in a well, from what moment does the water become impure? The two disciples apply the principle of certainty, while Abu Hanifa uses juristic preference to attribute impurity to an apparent cause. We will examine each view and its underlying reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Sahibayn hold impurity from the time of discovery (mudh wujid). Abu Hanifa distinguishes: if the animal is swollen or disintegrated, impurity from three days prior; if not swollen, from one day and night prior.
2
Note the proofs
Sahibayn base their view on qiyas (analogy): certainty is not removed by doubt, so impurity is judged from the nearest time (discovery). Abu Hanifa uses istihsan (juristic preference): attribution to the apparent cause (being in water) is necessary when causes are hidden.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in the Hanafi school.
3
Understand Abu Hanifa's conditions
Swelling indicates antiquity, so he estimates three days (the typical time for swelling). If not swollen, he estimates one day (since less than that is uncertain).
The estimation is based on customary occurrence (ghalabah).
4
Recognize the principle of 'attribution to the apparent cause'
Being in water is an apparent cause of impurity. When the actual timing is unknown, the law attributes the impurity to this apparent cause.
This is the basis of Abu Hanifa's istihsan.
5
See the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
If prayers were performed with the water, according to Sahibayn, only prayers after discovery need repetition. According to Abu Hanifa, depending on the estimated timeframe, more prayers might need repetition. Similarly for washing garments.
The practical consequence affects the number of repeated acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالا : مذ وجد. وسؤر :
وقالا : مذ وجد.
[۲]
وقت الوقوع يعيد الصلوات، ويغسل ما غسل منه، هذا إذا علم ؛ أي يقيناً أو ظنّاً وقت الوقوع ، وإن لم يعلم ذلك يحكم بنجاسته من وقت الوجود والاطلاع عليه عندهما مطلقا، وهو القياس ؛ لأنّ اليقين لا يزولُ بالشك.
والأصلُ إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته، وذلك لأنا تيقنا بطهارته فيما سبق ، ووقع الشك في نجاسته بعد ذلك، فلا يحكم به؛ لاحتمال أن يكون مات في غير البئر، ثم ألقتها الريح العاصف أو بعض السفهاء فيها.
وعند أبي حنيفة لله لو وجد منتفخاً أو متفسخاً يحكم بنجاستِهِ من ابتداء ثلاثةِ أيام ولياليها ؛ لأنّ الإحالة على السبب الظاهر واجب عند خفاء الأسباب، والكون في الماء سبب ظاهر فيحمل عليه، والانتفاخُ دليل التقادم فيقدر بالثلاث؛ لأنه يحصل في هذه المدة غالباً، وأما لو وجد غير منتفخ فيقدر عنده بيوم وليلة ; لأن ما دون ذلك ساعات غير منضبطة ، وفي المقام أبحاث مبسوطة في شروح «الهداية».
[١]قوله : وقالا مذ وجد ; أي ذلك النجس في البئر ، قال في «الجوهرة النيرة شرح مختصر القدوري»: «عليه الفتوى». انتهى. وفي «المجتبى»: «كان ركنُ الأئمة الصباغي يفتى بقول أبي حنيفة له فيما يتعلق بالصلاة ، وبقولهما فيما سواه، يعني في غسل الثوب والبدن والأواني وغير ذلك مما وصل إليه ذلك الماء».
And they two said: from the time it is found. And the remnant: And they two said: from the time it is found. [2] At the time of occurrence, he repeats the prayers, and washes what was washed from it; this is if he knows—i.e., certainly or conjecturally—the time of occurrence. And if he does not know that, it is judged impure from the time of existence and discovery according to them both absolutely, and that is analogy because certainty is not removed by doubt. The principle is to attribute the incident to the nearest of its times; because we were certain of its purity in the past, and doubt arose about its impurity after that, so it is not judged due to the possibility that it died outside the well and then a stormy wind or some foolish person threw it in. According to Abu Hanifa—may Allah be pleased with him—if it is found swollen or disintegrated, it is judged impure from the beginning of three days and their nights; because attribution to the apparent cause is necessary when causes are hidden, and being in the water is an apparent cause so it is attributed to it, and swelling is evidence of antiquity so it is estimated by three because it typically occurs within this period. But if it is found not swollen, it is estimated by him as one day and night because less than that are unregulated hours. In this regard, there are detailed investigations in the commentaries of Al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'And they two said: from the time it is found'—i.e., that impurity in the well. He said in Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: 'The fatwa is upon it.' End. And in Al-Mujtaba: 'Rukn al-A’immah al-Sabbaghi used to give fatwa according to Abu Hanifa’s statement regarding matters related to prayer, and according to their statement regarding other than that, meaning in washing the garment, body, vessels, and other things that this water reached.'
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the timing of water impurity when a dead animal is found in a well. The two disciples hold that the water is impure from the moment of discovery (mudh wujid), based on analogy (qiyas): certainty is not removed by doubt, and the principle is to attribute the event to the nearest time. They argue that since we were certain of purity before, and doubt arises only at discovery, impurity is judged from that time. Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the condition of the animal. If it is swollen or disintegrated, he considers it impure from three days prior, as swelling indicates antiquity and being in water is an apparent cause. If not swollen, he estimates one day and night. His position is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The commentary then notes that according to Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah, the fatwa is on the disciples' view, but Al-Mujtaba reports that Rukn al-A’immah al-Sabbaghi distinguished: for prayer-related matters, he gave fatwa on Abu Hanifa’s view (as it is more precautionary for worship), and for other matters (washing clothes, body, vessels), on the disciples' view.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning applied by the disciples to attribute impurity to the time of discovery based on the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger evidence or consideration; here, attributing impurity to the apparent cause (being in water) is preferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) in the passage, from which time is well water impure when a dead animal is found?
A) From three days prior if swollen
B) From the time of discovery (mudh wujid)
C) From one day prior if not swollen
D) It depends on the type of animal

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, when a dead animal is found in a well, the relied-upon view is that the water is impure from the time of discovery, but for prayer, Abu Hanifa's estimated times may be applied as a precaution.

8 min
The Ruling for Fatwa (Tarjih) and Practical Application
Tarjih and fatwa in the dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) ruling in the dispute on well impurity and explain the distinction between prayer and other matters as reported in Al-Mujtaba.

Core Idea
The fatwa is on the disciples' view, except for prayers where Abu Hanifa's view is followed for caution.

In addition to the dispute itself, the passage provides the later preference (tarjih) among the two views. According to Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah, the fatwa is on the disciples' view. However, Al-Mujtaba records that Rukn al-A’immah al-Sabbaghi gave fatwa according to Abu Hanifa for prayer-related matters and according to the disciples for other matters. This lesson explores this tarjih and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources of tarjih
The passage cites Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah (which holds the fatwa on the disciples' view) and Al-Mujtaba (which reports a distinction by Rukn al-A’immah al-Sabbaghi).
Both are later Hanafi authorities.
2
Note the general fatwa
Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah states: 'upon it (the disciples' view) is the fatwa'.
This is the default mufta bihi.
3
Note the exception for prayer
Al-Mujtaba reports that for matters related to prayer, Abu Hanifa's view is followed; for other matters (washing clothes, body, vessels), the disciples' view is followed.
This is a precautionary application (ihtiyat) for acts of worship.
4
Understand the reasoning
The precautionary principle (ihtiyat) is applied to prayer to ensure no invalid prayer is performed; hence the more demanding view is chosen.
In non-worship matters, the easier (disciples') view is adopted.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
If a person used the water for prayer only, the mufta bihi for prayer would require checking Abu Hanifa's estimated timing. If used for washing clothes, the disciples' view applies (impurity from discovery).
This distinction is key for fatwa giving.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالا : مذ وجد. وسؤر :
وقالا : مذ وجد.
[۲]
وقت الوقوع يعيد الصلوات، ويغسل ما غسل منه، هذا إذا علم ؛ أي يقيناً أو ظنّاً وقت الوقوع ، وإن لم يعلم ذلك يحكم بنجاسته من وقت الوجود والاطلاع عليه عندهما مطلقا، وهو القياس ؛ لأنّ اليقين لا يزولُ بالشك.
والأصلُ إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته، وذلك لأنا تيقنا بطهارته فيما سبق ، ووقع الشك في نجاسته بعد ذلك، فلا يحكم به؛ لاحتمال أن يكون مات في غير البئر، ثم ألقتها الريح العاصف أو بعض السفهاء فيها.
وعند أبي حنيفة لله لو وجد منتفخاً أو متفسخاً يحكم بنجاستِهِ من ابتداء ثلاثةِ أيام ولياليها ؛ لأنّ الإحالة على السبب الظاهر واجب عند خفاء الأسباب، والكون في الماء سبب ظاهر فيحمل عليه، والانتفاخُ دليل التقادم فيقدر بالثلاث؛ لأنه يحصل في هذه المدة غالباً، وأما لو وجد غير منتفخ فيقدر عنده بيوم وليلة ; لأن ما دون ذلك ساعات غير منضبطة ، وفي المقام أبحاث مبسوطة في شروح «الهداية».
[١]قوله : وقالا مذ وجد ; أي ذلك النجس في البئر ، قال في «الجوهرة النيرة شرح مختصر القدوري»: «عليه الفتوى». انتهى. وفي «المجتبى»: «كان ركنُ الأئمة الصباغي يفتى بقول أبي حنيفة له فيما يتعلق بالصلاة ، وبقولهما فيما سواه، يعني في غسل الثوب والبدن والأواني وغير ذلك مما وصل إليه ذلك الماء».
And they two said: from the time it is found. And the remnant: And they two said: from the time it is found. [2] At the time of occurrence, he repeats the prayers, and washes what was washed from it; this is if he knows—i.e., certainly or conjecturally—the time of occurrence. And if he does not know that, it is judged impure from the time of existence and discovery according to them both absolutely, and that is analogy because certainty is not removed by doubt. The principle is to attribute the incident to the nearest of its times; because we were certain of its purity in the past, and doubt arose about its impurity after that, so it is not judged due to the possibility that it died outside the well and then a stormy wind or some foolish person threw it in. According to Abu Hanifa—may Allah be pleased with him—if it is found swollen or disintegrated, it is judged impure from the beginning of three days and their nights; because attribution to the apparent cause is necessary when causes are hidden, and being in the water is an apparent cause so it is attributed to it, and swelling is evidence of antiquity so it is estimated by three because it typically occurs within this period. But if it is found not swollen, it is estimated by him as one day and night because less than that are unregulated hours. In this regard, there are detailed investigations in the commentaries of Al-Hidayah. [1] His statement: 'And they two said: from the time it is found'—i.e., that impurity in the well. He said in Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: 'The fatwa is upon it.' End. And in Al-Mujtaba: 'Rukn al-A’immah al-Sabbaghi used to give fatwa according to Abu Hanifa’s statement regarding matters related to prayer, and according to their statement regarding other than that, meaning in washing the garment, body, vessels, and other things that this water reached.'
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the timing of water impurity when a dead animal is found in a well. The two disciples hold that the water is impure from the moment of discovery (mudh wujid), based on analogy (qiyas): certainty is not removed by doubt, and the principle is to attribute the event to the nearest time. They argue that since we were certain of purity before, and doubt arises only at discovery, impurity is judged from that time. Abu Hanifa distinguishes based on the condition of the animal. If it is swollen or disintegrated, he considers it impure from three days prior, as swelling indicates antiquity and being in water is an apparent cause. If not swollen, he estimates one day and night. His position is based on istihsan (juristic preference). The commentary then notes that according to Al-Jawharah al-Nayrah, the fatwa is on the disciples' view, but Al-Mujtaba reports that Rukn al-A’immah al-Sabbaghi distinguished: for prayer-related matters, he gave fatwa on Abu Hanifa’s view (as it is more precautionary for worship), and for other matters (washing clothes, body, vessels), on the disciples' view.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning applied by the disciples to attribute impurity to the time of discovery based on the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger evidence or consideration; here, attributing impurity to the apparent cause (being in water) is preferred.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Mujtaba, on whose view is the fatwa for matters related to prayer?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Sahibayn
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school for well impurity is the disciples' view (from the time of discovery), but for prayer, the precautionary view of Abu Hanifa is sometimes adopted.

Definition of Su'ur and Ruling on Human Remnant
Definition and ruling on human su'ur

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define su'ur and state the Hanafi ruling on human su'ur, including the exception for an impure mouth.

Core Idea
The su'ur of every human is pure and purifying, except when the mouth is physically impure.

This lesson introduces the concept of su'ur (remnant) in Hanafi fiqh, focusing on the leftover liquid after drinking. The passage defines su'ur linguistically and then gives the ruling: all human su'ur is pure (tahir) and purifying (tahur), regardless of the person's state, unless the mouth is physically impure. Understanding this ruling is essential for daily purification practices.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its linguistic definition
The passage defines su'ur as the leftover liquid in a vessel after drinking, later extended to food. Note the pronunciation and orthography.
Su'ur has a dammah on the seen and a hamzated 'ayn.
2
State the general ruling on human su'ur
The su'ur of any human is pure (tahir) and purifying (tahur) without any dislike (karahah). This applies to all categories mentioned.
The ruling is general: male/female, young/old, Muslim/kafir, pure/junub, menstruating/postpartum.
3
Identify the exception to the general ruling
The exception occurs when the mouth is physically impure, e.g., immediately after drinking wine. In such a case, the su'ur is impure.
The impurity is due to the mouth, not the person's state of faith or ritual purity.
4
Understand the condition for purification after impurity
If a person waits an hour and swallows saliva three times, the mouth becomes pure, and the su'ur becomes pure again.
This detail is referenced from Al-Halabah Sharh al-Munyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : وسؤر ... الخ ; لما كان بعض مسائل الآبار متوقفة على مسائل الآثار، ذكر أحكام السؤر بعد أحكام البئر، وهو بضم السين ، مهموز العين : اسم للبقية بعد الشراب التي أبقاها الشارب في الإناء، ثمّ عمّ استعماله في الطعام وغيره.
الآدمي والفرس، وكل ما يأكل لحمه طاهر والكلبُ
[1]
الآدمي والفرس ، وكل ما يأكل لحمه طاهر ) والكلب)
١ ] قوله : الآدمي ; قدمه لشرافته، وأطلقه فشمل الذكر والأنثى، والصغير والكبير والمسلم والكافر، والطاهر والجنب، والحائض والنفساء، فإنّ سؤر الكل طاهر وطهور من غير كراهة ، إلا أن يكون فمه نجساً ، فسؤر شارب خمر فور شربها نجس، بخلاف ما إذا مكث ساعة وابتلع ريقه ثلاث مرات، كما في «الحلبة شرح المنية».
[2] His statement: 'And the remnant...etc.'; because some issues of wells depend on issues of remnants, he mentioned the rulings of su’ur after the rulings of the well. Su’ur—with a dammah on the seen, hamzated in the ‘ayn—is a name for the leftover after drinking that the drinker left in the vessel; then its usage generalised to food and other things. The human, the horse, and every animal whose flesh is eaten—its su’ur is pure—and the dog. [1] His statement: 'The human'; he mentioned it first due to its honour, and he spoke generally, including male and female, young and old, Muslim and non-Muslim, pure and junub, menstruating and postpartum; the su’ur of all is pure and purifying without dislike, except if his mouth is impure, so the su’ur of a wine drinker immediately after drinking is impure, contrary to when he waits an hour and swallows his saliva three times, as in Al-Halabah Sharh al-Munyah.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage first defines the term 'su’ur' (remnant) linguistically: it is the leftover liquid in a vessel after someone drinks, later extended to food. Then it presents the ruling on human su’ur: it is pure (tahir) and purifying (tahur) without any dislike (karahah). This applies to all humans regardless of age, gender, faith, or state of ritual purity. The only exception is if the mouth is physically impure, e.g., a person who just drank wine—then the su’ur is impure. However, if after drinking wine one waits an hour and swallows saliva three times, the mouth becomes pure again. This ruling is from the Hanafi school; the commentary references 'Al-Halabah Sharh al-Munyah' for this detail.

Key Terms
سؤر (su’ur): The leftover liquid in a vessel after a person drinks; extended to food remains.
طاهر وطهور (tahir wa tahur): Pure in itself and purifying for others; can be used for wudu and ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the person is in a state of janabah
B) When the person is a non-Muslim
C) Immediately after drinking wine
D) When the person is menstruating

Key Takeaway
All human su’ur is pure and purifying except when the mouth is physically impure, in which case the su’ur is impure until the mouth becomes pure again (e.g., by waiting and swallowing saliva).

Four Narrations on Horse Su'ur: Resolving Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on horse su'ur with resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four narrations from Abu Hanifa on horse su'ur and explain why the pure view is preferred.

Core Idea
Horse su'ur is pure despite the prohibition of its flesh, because the prohibition is for honour, not impurity.

In this passage, the commentator presents four different rulings attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa regarding the purity of water left by a horse (su'ur al-faras). Such internal differences are not uncommon in the Hanafi school, as narrators transmitted different statements from the Imam. The commentary then guides us to the correct, relied-upon position, providing a reasoned basis for the preference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What exactly is being debated? The purity of water left by a horse (su'ur) and its permissibility for wudu.
The issue is specific to the Hanafi school's internal transmission.
2
List the four transmitted views
From Abu Hanifa: (1) prefer other water, (2) makruh like its flesh, (3) doubtful like donkey su'ur, (4) pure like the disciples' view.
Only the fourth is declared correct.
3
Understand the basis of each view
Each narration likely analogizes horse su'ur to another object: caution, meat, donkey, or human su'ur.
The fourth uses human su'ur as an analogy.
4
Recognize the preferred view and its evidence
The fourth narration is 'as-sahih' because the prohibition of horse flesh is for honour (to glorify the horse in jihad), not impurity, so su'ur remains pure like human su'ur.
This is the mufta bihi view.
5
Apply the preferred view in practice
A Hanafi following the relied-upon view can use water drunk by a horse for wudu without any dislike.
The ruling is pure and permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : والفرس ; قال في «النهاية» و«البناية»: سؤر الفرس طاهر في ظاهر الرواية، وروي عن أبي حنيفة له أربع روايات :
۱. فروى البلخي له أنه قال : أحب إلي أن يتوضأ بغيره.
٢. وروى الحَسَنُ له عنه له أنه مكروه كلحمه.
وروی أنه مشكوك كسور الحمار.
٤. وروى عنه أنه طاهر كقولهما ، وهو الصحيح; لأنّ كراهة لحمه عنده لإظهار شرفه ; لأنه يرهب به عدو الله، فيقعُ به إعزاز الدين، فلا يؤثر تحريمه في سؤره كما في الآدمي.
[2] His statement: 'And the horse;' he said in Al-Nihayah and Al-Binayah: The su’ur of the horse is pure according to the apparent narration. And it is narrated from Abu Hanifa—may Allah be pleased with him—four narrations: 1. Al-Balkhi narrated from him that he said: I prefer that one perform wudu with other than it. 2. Al-Hasan narrated from him that it is disliked like its flesh. And it was narrated that it is doubtful like the su’ur of the donkey. 4. And it was narrated from him that it is pure like their statement (the two disciples), and that is the correct one; because the prohibition of its flesh according to him is to manifest its honour, because it terrorises the enemy of Allah, and by it the religion is glorified, so its prohibition does not affect its su’ur just as in the case of a human.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage discusses the su’ur (remnant) of a horse within the Hanafi school. The text states that in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah), horse su’ur is pure. Then it mentions four different narrations attributed to Abu Hanifa himself: (1) a narration from Al-Balkhi that Abu Hanifa preferred to use other water for wudu (implying tanzihan); (2) a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad that it is disliked (makruh) like horse flesh; (3) a narration that it is doubtful (mashkuk) like donkey su’ur; and (4) a narration that it is pure (tahir) like the view of the two disciples, which is declared the correct one. The reasoning given is that the prohibition of eating horse flesh in the Hanafi school is not due to impurity but to honour the horse as a war animal for jihad, so its saliva and su’ur remain pure, analogous to the human su’ur (which is pure despite the human's sacredness not being inherently purifying). Thus, the relied-upon position is that horse su’ur is pure.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The apparent or well-known transmission of the school; here it states horse su’ur is pure.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked; in one narration, using horse su’ur for wudu is disliked (tanzihan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which view on horse su'ur is considered 'as-sahih' (correct) according to the passage?
A) It is disliked
B) It is doubtful
C) It is pure like the disciples' view
D) It is preferable to use other water

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon view is that horse su'ur is pure, as the prohibition of its flesh is for honour, not impurity.

Ruling on Su'ur of Halal Animals and the Exception for Jalalah
Ruling on su'ur of halal animals and exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on su'ur of halal animals, identify when it becomes disliked due to jalalah, and explain the ruling for animals without flowing blood.

Core Idea
Su'ur of halal animals is pure; it becomes disliked only if the animal is a habitual filth-eater.

This lesson examines the purity of the leftover water or saliva (su'ur) from animals whose flesh is lawful to eat. The Hanafi school generally holds that such su'ur is pure and can be used for wudu. However, there is an important exception for 'jalalah' animals that regularly consume filth. The passage also extends the ruling to aquatic animals without flowing blood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Find the statement that the su'ur of every animal whose flesh is eaten (e.g., sheep) is pure.
The ruling is unconditional except for an incidental reason.
2
Recognize the exception for jalalah
Note that camels, cows, and chickens that eat filth are excepted; their su'ur is disliked (makruh).
Jalalah means the animal habitually eats impure substances.
3
Understand the basis for purity
The passage explains that su'ur is mixed with saliva, and the saliva of humans, horses, and edible animals is pure by consensus.
This is the underlying rationale.
4
Apply the extension to water creatures
Animals without flowing blood that live in water are attached to the ruling of edible animals; their su'ur is pure.
Examples: fish, frogs (but not all aquatic life).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣]قوله : وكلّ ما يؤكل لحمه ; أي سؤر ما يُباحُ أكل لحمه بالذبح كالشاة ونحوها طاهر من غير كراهة ، إلا لأمر عارض كما في سؤر الإبل الجلالة والبقر الجلالة، وهي التي تأكل العذرة، وكذا الدجاجة المخلاة المختلطة بأكل النجاسات، فإنّه مكروه، كما في «البحر» (١) ، وغيره. [٤] قوله : طاهر ; أي بلا كراهة كما يقتضيه التقابل بالمكروه ; وذلك لأنَّ السؤر مخلوط باللعاب، ولعاب الإنسان والفرس ومأكول اللحم طاهر بالإجماع، ويلحق بمأكول اللحم ما ليس له نفس سائلة ما يعيش في الماء، فإنّ سؤره طاهر، ذكره الزيلعي في تبيين الحقائق شرح كنز الدقائق
[3] His statement: 'And every animal whose flesh is eaten;' i.e., the su’ur of what is permissible to eat by slaughter, like sheep and the like, is pure without dislike, except for an incidental reason, as in the su’ur of jalalah camels and jalalah cows, which eat filth, and similarly the free-ranging chicken mixed with eating impurities, then it is disliked, as in Al-Bahr and others. [4] His statement: 'Pure;' i.e., without dislike, as implied by the contrast with the disliked; and that is because su’ur is mixed with saliva, and the saliva of humans, horses, and animals whose meat is eaten is pure by consensus. And attached to meat-eating animals is that which has no flowing blood, living in water, for its su’ur is pure, mentioned by al-Zayla’i in Tabyin al-Haqa’iq Sharh Kanz al-Daqa’iq.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage states the general ruling: the su’ur (remnant) of animals whose flesh is lawfully eaten (e.g., sheep, cow, goat) is pure (tahir) and can be used for wudu without any dislike. The purity extends to the saliva mixed with the su’ur. However, there is an exception for 'jalalah' animals—those that habitually eat filth (like humans' waste). The su’ur of such animals (camels, cows, and free-ranging chickens that eat impurities) is disliked (makruh). Additionally, the passage mentions that animals without flowing blood that live in water (e.g., fish) are attached to the same ruling: their su’ur is pure. This is based on the principle that the saliva of a pure animal is pure, and the ruling is by consensus (ijma) for the listed animals.

Key Terms
جلالة (jalalah): An animal that habitually eats filth (najis), such as a camel or cow that eats human waste.
ما لا نفس له سائلة (ma la nafsa lahu sa'ilah): Animals that have no flowing blood (e.g., fish, insects that live in water); their su’ur is considered pure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Always pure
B) When it is a jalalah (eats filth)
C) If it is slaughtered
D) If it lives in water

Key Takeaway
The su'ur of halal animals is pure, but becomes disliked if the animal is a habitual filth-eater (jalalah).

Dog Su'ur is Impure for Purification
Partial sharh ruling on dog su'ur impurity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the impurity of dog su'ur and identify its practical effect on purification.

Core Idea
Dog su'ur is impure (najis) and cannot be used for purification in the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines the ruling on the leftover water or liquid (su'ur) of a dog. The passage introduces the impurity of dog su'ur, a core ruling in Hanafi taharah. Understanding this helps you avoid invalidating your wudu or ghusl when water has been contaminated by a dog's mouth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The passage begins by identifying the dog as the subject: 'And the dog; it, along with its two companions, is conjoined to the human.' Determine that the ruling applies to the su'ur (residual water or liquid) of the dog.
'Its two companions' likely refers to the pig and something else, but the text is incomplete.
2
Extract the ruling
The phrase 'the aspect in the impurity of its su'ur' clearly indicates that the su'ur of the dog is impure (najis). The ruling is that dog su'ur is najis, not just disliked.
The Hanafi school considers the dog's saliva and any liquid it touches as impure.
3
Apply the ruling practically
If a dog drinks from a vessel, the remaining water is impure and cannot be used for wudu, ghusl, or drinking. The vessel must be washed seven times, one with earth, according to the Hanafi school.
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) also consider dog saliva impure, but may have different washing requirements.
4
Distinguish from other animals
The dog is distinguished from humans and other animals whose su'ur is generally pure. The impurity of dog su'ur is specific due to its inherent najasah.
The su'ur of cats and other pets is pure in the Hanafi school unless there is visible impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[0] قوله : والكلب ; هو مع قرينيه معطوف على الآدمي، والوجه في نجاسة سؤر
[0] His statement: 'And the dog;' it, along with its two companions, is conjoined to 'the human', and the aspect in the impurity of its su’ur
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion on the su’ur of the dog. It states that the word 'dog' is conjoined to 'human' in the original matn, and then begins to explain the reason (wajh) for its impurity. However, the text is incomplete in this window. The complete ruling is that the su’ur of the dog is impure (najis) in the Hanafi school, and it does not purify water or other liquids. The incomplete sentence was likely going to elaborate on the basis for this ruling.

Key Terms
سؤر (su'ur): The leftover water or liquid after an animal drinks.
نجاسة (najasah): Impurity; here, the su’ur of the dog is impure.
كلب (kalb): Dog; an animal whose su'ur is impure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ruling on Su'ur of Humans, Horses, and Edible Animals
Expansion on su'ur rulings with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on su'ur for humans, horses, and edible animals, identify the relied-upon position on horse su'ur amid intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, and apply the jallalah exception.

Core Idea
Human su'ur is always pure; horse su'ur is pure in the relied-upon view; edible animal su'ur is pure unless jallalah.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details the Hanafi rulings on su'ur (leftover water) based on the source animals. The commentator starts with humans, affirming purity without exception unless the mouth is contaminated, then addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute over horse su'ur, resolving it with the soundest narration. Finally, edible animals are covered, noting the exception for jallalah animals that feed on filth. The reasoning emphasizes that purity is tied to the animal's essence, not its meat's permissibility.

Guided Path
1
State the base ruling for human su'ur
Identify that human su'ur is tahir and tahur without karahah, covering all types of humans. Note the exception: if the mouth is contaminated (e.g., wine immediately after drinking), the su'ur becomes najis.
The commentator includes men, women, children, Muslims, disbelievers, junub, menstruating, and postpartum women.
2
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on horse su'ur
Recognize that in Zahir al-Riwayah horse su'ur is pure, but there are four narrations from Abu Hanifa: (1) prefers using other water, (2) makruh like its meat, (3) doubtful like donkey su'ur, (4) pure like the Sahibayn. The commentator declares the fourth as sahih.
The tarjih rests on the reasoning that horse meat is prohibited for honor (to strike terror), not because of impurity, so it does not affect the su'ur.
3
Apply the ruling to edible animals
State that su'ur of animals whose meat is halal (e.g., sheep) is pure without dislike, unless the animal is jallalah (feeds on filth). For jallalah, the su'ur is makruh until it is cleansed.
The commentator extends the ruling to creatures with no flowing blood (e.g., fish), whose su'ur is pure by consensus.
4
Identify the mufta bihi position
Confirm that the relied-upon Hanafi position follows the fourth narration: horse su'ur is pure, aligning with Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. Human su'ur is pure unconditionally except for the mouth contamination. Edible animal su'ur is pure unless jallalah.
This resolution is based on the maxim that the prohibition of horse meat is not due to najasah.

كتاب الطهارة / الطهارات، والآبار، والآسار الآدمي والفرس، وكل ما يأكل لحمه طاهر والكلبُ [1] قوله : الآدمي ؛ قدمه لشرافته، وأطلقه فشمل الذكر والأنثى، والصغير والكبير والمسلم والكافر، والطاهر والجنب، والحائض والنفساء، فإنّ سؤر الكل طاهر وطهور من غير كراهة ، إلا أن يكون فمه نجساً ، فسؤر شارب خمر فور شربها نجس، بخلاف ما إذا مكث ساعة وابتلع ريقه ثلاث مرات، كما في «الحلبة شرح المنية». The chapter of purification / Purifications, wells, and remnants. The human and the horse, and everything whose meat is eaten, its su'ur is pure. [1] His saying: 'the human' — he gave it precedence for its nobility, and he used it unrestrictedly so it includes male and female, young and old, Muslim and disbeliever, pure and junub, menstruating and postpartum, for the su'ur of all is pure and purifying without dislike, except if his mouth is impure, then the su'ur of a wine drinker immediately after drinking is impure, contrary to if he waited an hour and swallowed his saliva three times, as in 'al-Hulbah Sharh al-Munyah'. [2] His saying: 'and the horse' — it is said in 'al-Nihayah' and 'al-Binayah': the su'ur of the horse is pure in the apparent narration, and it is narrated from Abu Hanifah four narrations: 1. al-Balkhi narrated from him that he said: I prefer that you perform wudu with other water. 2. al-Hasan narrated from him that it is disliked like its meat. 3. It is narrated that it is doubtful like the su'ur of the donkey. 4. It is narrated from him that it is pure like their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) statement, and that is the correct one, because the dislike of its meat according to him is for manifesting its honor, for it strikes terror into the enemy of Allah, and through it the religion is dignified, so its prohibition does not affect its su'ur, as in the case of the human. [3] His saying: 'and everything whose meat is eaten' — i.e., the su'ur of what is permissible to eat by slaughter like sheep and similar is pure without dislike, except for an incidental reason as in the su'ur of jallalah camels and jallalah cows, which eat filth, and likewise the free-roaming hen that mixes with eating impurities, for it is disliked, as in 'al-Bahr' (1) and others. [4] His saying: 'pure' — i.e., without dislike, as is required by the contrast with what is disliked; because the su'ur is mixed with saliva, and the saliva of humans, horses, and edible meat is pure by consensus, and what has no flowing blood and lives in water is attached to edible meat, for its su'ur is pure, mentioned by al-Zayla'i in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq Sharh Kanz al-Daqa'iq ((2)).
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn stating that the su'ur (leftover water) of humans, horses, and all animals whose meat is eaten is pure. The commentator begins with humans, affirming that their su'ur is tahir (pure) and tahur (purifying) without any dislike, covering all types of humans except when the mouth is contaminated with najasah (like wine immediately after drinking). For horses, he notes that in the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) it is pure, but there are four narrations from Abu Hanifa: preferring other water, considering it makruh, doubtful, or pure. The commentator declares the fourth (pure) to be the sahih (correct) position, reasoning that the prohibition of horse meat is for honor (to strike terror into enemies of Allah), not because of impurity, so it does not affect the su'ur. For edible animals, the ruling is pure without dislike, except when the animal is 'jallalah' (feeds on filth) — such as camels, cows, or free-roaming hens that eat impurities; their su'ur is makruh. The commentator also extends the ruling to creatures with no flowing blood (like fish) and those living in water, saying their su'ur is pure by consensus.

Key Terms
سؤر (su'r): The water left over after a person or animal drinks, or the remnant of water in a vessel.
الآدمي (al-adami): The human being, including all types regardless of gender, age, religion, or state of purity.
الفرس (al-faras): The horse, whose su'ur is ruled as tahir in the relied-upon Hanafi position.
جلالة (jallalah): An animal that feeds on filth and impurities, such that its meat and milk become affected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Najis
B) Makruh
C) Tahir (pure)
D) Mashkuk (doubtful)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon view: human su'ur is pure unconditionally (except mouth contamination), horse su'ur is pure, and edible animal su'ur is pure unless the animal is jallalah.

Ruling on Su'ur of Dog, Pig, and Beasts of Prey
Proof that su'ur of dog, pig, and beasts of prey is najis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the su'ur of dog, pig, and beasts of prey and explain the two proofs (hadith and qiyas) used to derive it.

Core Idea
The su'ur of dog, pig, and beasts of prey is najis because their saliva is impure, proven by hadith and analogy from their prohibited meat.

In this passage, the commentator explains why the leftover water (su'ur) of certain animals is legally impure. The ruling is based on the impurity of their saliva, which is established by direct hadith evidence and by analogical reasoning (qiyas) linking saliva to meat. Understanding this ruling is essential for daily purification (taharah) in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the animals and the ruling
The passage states that the su'ur of the dog, pig, and beasts of prey (e.g., lion, wolf) is najis (impure). This is the core ruling.
Beasts of prey are land animals that hunt with teeth.
2
Examine the first proof: hadith
The author cites hadiths that command washing a garment soiled by their saliva. This indicates that the saliva is impure, and since su'ur is mixed with saliva, it too is impure.
The hadiths are not quoted directly but are referenced as 'ahadith'.
3
Examine the second proof: qiyas from meat
Saliva is generated from the animal's meat. Since the meat is impure (proven by the prohibition of eating it, which is not for honor), the saliva is also impure by analogy.
This is a qiyas jaliy (clear analogy).
4
Note the additional proof for the pig
The pig is additionally najis al-ayn (inherently impure) in all its parts, making its su'ur even more clearly impure.
This is an additional reason unique to the pig.
5
Confirm the Hanafi position and note cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi school holds all three as najis. The Shafi'i school agrees on dog and pig but considers beasts of prey pure.
This difference affects practical rulings on water purity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلب والخنزير وسباع البهائم نجس [0] قوله : والكلب ; هو مع قرينيه معطوف على الآدمي، والوجه في نجاسة سؤر هذه الثلاثة ؛ أي الكلب والخنزير وسباع البهائم أنّ سؤرها مخلوط بلعابها، ولعابها نجس، بدليل ورود الأحاديث الدالة على غسل ثوب أصابه لعابها، وتنجس ماء أصابه لعابها. وبدليل أنه متولد من لحومها وهي نجسة بدليل تحريم أكلها، فإنّ الحرمة لا لكرامة آية النجاسة، ويزادُ عليه في الخنزير كونه نجس العين بجميع أجزائه.
And the dog, pig, and beasts of prey are impure. [0] His saying: 'and the dog' — it is with its two companions conjoined to 'the human'. The reason for the impurity of the su'ur of these three: that their su'ur is mixed with their saliva, and their saliva is impure, as evidenced by the hadiths indicating that a garment soiled by their saliva must be washed, and water soiled by their saliva becomes impure. And also by evidence that it is generated from their meat, which is impure as proven by the prohibition of eating it, for prohibition (when not for honor) is a sign of impurity. And in the case of the pig, it is additionally that it is inherently impure in all its parts.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage states the ruling that the su'ur (leftover water) of dogs, pigs, and beasts of prey (such as lions and wolves) is najis (impure). The commentator explains the reasoning: the su'ur is mixed with saliva, and the saliva of these animals is impure. This is proven by two proofs: first, hadiths that require washing a garment or water that comes into contact with their saliva, indicating impurity; second, the saliva is generated from their meat, and their meat is impure because it is prohibited (and the prohibition is not for honor, unlike horse), so the generated saliva is also impure. For the pig, there is an additional element: it is najis al-ayn (inherently impure) in all its parts. Therefore, their su'ur cannot be used for purification and will render impure anything it contacts.

Key Terms
سباع البهائم (siba' al-baha'im): Beasts of prey from among land animals, such as lions, wolves, tigers, etc.
نجس (najis): Legally impure, such that it prevents the validity of prayer if present on the body or clothes, and nullifies the purity of water.
لعاب (lu'ab): Saliva or slobber of an animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because it is not eaten

Key Takeaway
The su'ur of dog, pig, and beasts of prey is najis based on hadith and qiyas from their impure meat, a key ruling in Hanafi taharah.

Ruling on the Su'ur of the Cat, Free-Roaming Hen, Birds of Prey, and Domestic Creatures
Definition and explanation of rulings on su'ur of specific animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the su'ur of these animals and identify the condition that lifts the karahah.

Core Idea
The su'ur of cat and similar animals is not impure, but using it is disliked if other water exists.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines and explains the ruling on the su'ur (leftover water) of four categories of animals: the cat, the free-roaming hen, birds of prey, and domestic creatures. While their meat may be prohibited, the hadith clarifies that the cat is not najis, yet the Hanafi school deems its su'ur makruh tanzihi due to the likelihood of contact with impurities. Importantly, the karahah is conditional: it applies only when one has access to other water; otherwise, it is perfectly permissible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the categories
List the four categories of animals mentioned: cat, free-roaming hen, birds of prey, and domestic creatures.
Each has the same ruling but with different reasoning.
2
Understand the key distinction
Note the difference between the free-roaming hen (mukhallah) and the confined hen (mahbusah) – the latter's su'ur is not makruh.
This shows the 'illa (cause) for karahah is diet.
3
Grasp the conditional nature
The karahah applies only when alternative water is available; if not, there is no karahah.
This is a key point: the ruling changes with necessity.
4
Understand the underlying reason
The cat's meat is haram, which would normally imply impurity, but the hadith overrides it, resulting in pure but makruh.
This illustrates the interaction of dalils in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the cat, the free-roaming hen, birds of prey, and domestic creatures are makruh. [1] His saying: 'and the cat' — it is with what follows conjoined to 'the human', and the predicate is 'makruh'. The cat (hirrah) with a shaddah on the ra', before it a kasrah on the ha', plural: hirar, like qirdah and qird, in Persian: gurbah. 'The free-roaming hen' (al-dajajah al-mukhallah) is a passive participle from takhliyah, meaning the one let loose, roaming, eating from filth and impurities. The condition 'al-mukhallah' excludes the confined one (al-mahbusah), which is kept in a place and fed there, so its su'ur is not makruh because there is no likelihood of it mixing with impurities. 'Birds of prey' (siba' al-tayr) are birds that hunt with their beaks and prey, like the falcon and hawk. 'Domestic creatures' (sawakin al-buyut) are insects and animals that dwell in houses, like the mouse, snake, scorpion, gecko, and so on. It is the plural of sakinah, and the feminine is used because most are expressed in the feminine, or it is the plural of sakin, and the plural of fa'il on fawa'il in attributes is qiyasi according to some absolutely, and according to some in the case of non-intelligent beings. [2] His saying: 'makruh' — so it is permissible to perform wudu with it and use it with karahah if one has access to other water; if one does not have other water, then there is no karahah. The reason for the karahah: as for the su'ur of the cat, the prohibition of its meat, although it would require its impurity, which would entail the impurity of its saliva, which would entail the impurity of its su'ur, this is dropped due to the hadith: 'The cat is not...'
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling on the su'ur (leftover water) of four categories: cat, free-roaming hen, birds of prey (like falcon), and domestic creatures (like mice, snakes). The matn declares their su'ur as 'makruh' (disliked). The commentator defines each term and explains the reasoning for the karahah. For the cat, by default the prohibition of its meat would imply impurity, but the hadith 'The cat is not impure' overrides this, resulting in makruh rather than najis. The free-roaming hen's su'ur is makruh because it eats filth; if it is confined and fed clean food, its su'ur is not makruh. For birds of prey and domestic creatures, the karahah is due to their likely contact with impurities. Importantly, the karahah is conditional: if one has other water for wudu, it is disliked to use this su'ur; but if no other water is available, the karahah is lifted and it is permissible to use it without dislike.

Key Terms
هرة (hirrah): The domestic cat. Its su'ur is ruled as makruh due to the hadith that it is not impure.
دجاجة مخلاة (dajajah mukhallah): A hen that is let free to roam and eat from impurities and filth.
سباع الطير (siba' al-tayr): Birds of prey that hunt with their beaks, such as falcons and hawks.
سواكن البيوت (sawakin al-buyut): Creatures that dwell in houses, such as mice, snakes, scorpions, geckos, etc.
مكروه (makruh): Disliked (tanzihan); using this su'ur for wudu is permissible but less rewarded if other water is available.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on the su'ur of the cat, free-roaming hen, birds of prey, and domestic creatures is makruh tanzihi, but the karahah is conditional upon the availability of other water for wudu.
