Tayammum for Janaba: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i on Wudu
Ruling and inter-madhhab dispute on tayammum for janaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tayammum for a person in janaba with water insufficient for ghusl, explain the Shafi'i objection based on qiyas, and apply the distinction between tangible and legal purification to resolve the dispute.

Core Idea
Legal purification (tahāra hukmiyya) depends on capacity for complete purification; partial water does not trigger a partial obligation.

This passage from 'Umda al-Ri'aya presents a classic inter-madhhab dispute: if a person in janaba has only enough water for wudu but not for full ghusl, must he perform wudu first? The Hanafi school answers no—tayammum for ghusl suffices directly. The Shafi'i school analogizes to covering partial nakedness or using limited water for physical impurity. The key lies in distinguishing tangible removal from legal purification, a fundamental usul difference that produces different rulings.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: a junub with water insufficient for ghusl but present for wudu performs tayammum for ghusl without prior wudu. Note that the Hanafis treat this as wajib (obligatory) because tayammum substitutes for the missed purification.
The passage begins with 'He performs tayammum for ghusl, and wudu is not obligatory upon him according to us.'
2
Identify the opposing school and its proof
The Shafi'i school opposes: wudu is wajib, then tayammum for ghusl. Their proof is qiyas: like a naked person covering part of his 'awra, or using limited water to remove physical impurity from a garment. The analogy is that partial capacity triggers partial obligation.
The passage mentions: 'like a naked person who finds a garment covering part of his nakedness, it is incumbent upon him to cover that much.'
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes tangible removal (hissī) from legal purification (hukmī). Physical impurity requires maximal tangible removal, so partial water must be used. But legal purification (hadath) is a ruling contingent on the capacity to complete the entire purification. Since water is insufficient for the obligatory ghusl, the condition for wudu (finding sufficient water) is not met, so tayammum is permitted directly.
The passage says: 'What is removed there is a tangible matter, so removal is considered tangibly... but here purification is legal.'
4
Clarify a textual nuance
The phrase 'if with janaba there is an event requiring wudu' is interpreted by the commentator to mean 'after tayammum for janaba, if a subsequent hadath occurs.' This shows that if a new minor hadath arises after tayammum, wudu becomes obligatory for that new hadath, not for the original janaba. The earlier tayammum covers janaba only.
The passage explains that 'ma'a' (with) here means 'after' (ba'd), and the annexed element is tayammum.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafi says tayammum only; Shafi'i says wudu + tayammum. Also, when a new hadath occurs after tayammum for janaba, both schools agree wudu is needed for the new hadath, but the Hanafi position on the initial scenario remains distinct.
The thamarah directly affects what actions the person performs before prayer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا للغسل يتيمم ولا يجب عليه التوضؤ عندنا خلافاً للشافعي. وأما إذا كان مع الجنابة حدث يوجب الوضوء يجب عليه الوضوء فالتيمم للجنابة بالاتفاق. وأما إذا كان للمحدث ماء يكفي لغسل بعض أعضائه فالخلاف ثابت أيضاً. قوله: يتيمم؛ أي يجب عليه أن يتيمم للغسل؛ لأنه وإن وجد ماء لكن لم يجد ماء كافياً للطهارة الواجبة عليه، والمصير عند فقده إلى التيمم. قوله: خلافاً للشافعي؛ فإنه يقول بوجوب التوضؤ ثم التيمم للغسل، كالعاري إذا وجد ثوباً يستر بعض عورته يلزمه ستر ذلك القدر، وكذا إذا كانت بمن وجد ماءً نجاسة حقيقية بثوبه أو بدنه يجب عليه استعماله في ذلك القدر. ونحن نقول: المزال هناك أمر حسي فاعتبر الزوال حساً فوجب الاستعمال ما أمكن، وأما هاهنا فالطهارة حكمية، ووجوب الوضوء والغسل منوط بوجدان الماء الكافي لطهارته بحيث تحل بها الصلاة، فإذا لم يوجد يباح له التيمم. قوله: أما إذا كان مع الجنابة... الخ؛ ظاهر هذه العبارة مختل... والقول المعتمد في هذا المقام أن كلمة "مع" بمعنى "بعد"، والمضاف محذوف، أو هي بمعناها والمضاف محذوف وهو التيمم. وحاصله: أنه إذا وجد الجنب قدراً كافياً للوضوء قبل التيمم لا يجب عليه الوضوء عندنا، وأما إذا تيمم بالجنابة ثم طرأ عليه ناقض الوضوء ووجد ماء كافياً له يجب عليه الوضوء عندنا أيضاً، فإن التيمم السابق للجنابة لا يرتفع به الحدث الطارئ. وبه ظهر أن الفاء تعليلية، والمقصود به رفع ما يقال من أنه كيف يجب عليه الوضوء مع بقاء التيمم بأن التيمم السابق إنما هو للجنابة لا للحدث الذي حدث بعده، وقوله: "بالاتفاق" متعلق بوجوب الوضوء أو بكون التيمم للجنابة اتفاقاً، وإنما وجب الوضوء للحدث الطارئ، ومن اختار أن معناه التيمم للجنابة واجب بعد الوضوء فقد أخطأ.
He performs tayammum for ghusl, and wudu is not obligatory upon him according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. And if with janaba there is an event requiring wudu, wudu is obligatory, then tayammum for janaba by agreement. And if the one in hadath has water sufficient for washing some of his limbs, the disagreement also remains. His statement: 'he performs tayammum' means it is obligatory upon him to perform tayammum for ghusl because even though he found water, he did not find water sufficient for the purification obligatory upon him, and the recourse when it is absent is tayammum. His statement: 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' – for he says wudu is obligatory, then tayammum for ghusl, like a naked person who finds a garment covering part of his nakedness, it is incumbent upon him to cover that much; similarly, if one finds water and has a real impurity on his garment or body, he must use it for that amount. And we say: what is removed there is a tangible matter, so removal is considered tangibly, so use is obligatory as much as possible; but here purification is legal, and the obligation of wudu and ghusl is contingent on finding water sufficient for his purification such that prayer becomes permissible with it. If it is not found, tayammum is permitted for him. His statement: 'And if with janaba... etc.' – the apparent wording is problematic... and the relied-upon statement in this place is that the word 'ma'a' means 'after', and the annexed is omitted, or it is with its meaning and the annexed is omitted, which is tayammum. Its summary: if the junub finds an amount sufficient for wudu before tayammum, wudu is not obligatory upon him according to us. And if he performed tayammum for janaba, then a nullifier of wudu occurs and he finds water sufficient for it, wudu becomes obligatory upon him according to us as well, for the previous tayammum for janaba does not remove the subsequent hadath. By this it becomes clear that the 'fa' is for explanation, and the intended meaning is to rebut what might be said: how can wudu be obligatory while tayammum remains? That the previous tayammum is only for janaba, not for the hadath that occurred after it. And his statement: 'by agreement' is attached to the obligation of wudu or to the fact that tayammum for janaba is agreed upon. Wudu is only obligatory due to the subsequent hadath, and whoever chooses that its meaning is that tayammum for janaba is obligatory after wudu has erred.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and inter-madhhab dispute from 'Umda al-Ri'aya. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: a person in a state of major ritual impurity (janaba) who has only enough water for ghusl (full body wash) but not for both wudu and ghusl is not required to perform wudu separately; rather, he performs tayammum directly for the ghusl. This is contrary to the Shafi'i school, which holds that if such a person has enough water for wudu, he must first perform wudu and then tayammum for the ghusl. The author then explains the case where a person has both janaba and a minor hadath (e.g., needing wudu) together: then he must perform wudu, and the tayammum for janaba is by agreement. Then the author elaborates on the Shafi'i analogy: like a naked person covering part of his 'awra, or a person with a limited amount of water to remove physical impurity. The Hanafi response distinguishes physical impurity (which requires physical removal) and legal purification (which is a ruling based on the capacity to complete the purification). The key principle: the obligation of wudu or ghusl is contingent on finding water sufficient for the entire purification that makes prayer valid. If insufficient, tayammum is permitted. Then the author clarifies a textual issue: the phrase "Ammā idhā kāna ma'a al-janāba..." is problematic, and the preferred interpretation is that "ma'a" here means "after" (ba'd), and the missing element is tayammum. So the meaning: if after performing tayammum for janaba, another hadath occurs, then wudu becomes obligatory when water is found. The previous tayammum does not remove the new hadath. Thus, the dispute is resolved.

Key Terms
طهارة حكمية (tahāra hukmiyya): Legal purification that is a ruling of the Law, not a physical removal of impurity; it is achieved by wudu, ghusl, or tayammum as substitutes.
جنابة (janāba): Major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, caused by sexual intercourse or seminal discharge.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution using pure earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or cannot be used.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person in janaba has water sufficient for wudu but not for ghusl, what is the ruling?
A) He must perform wudu then tayammum for ghusl.
B) He performs tayammum directly for ghusl without wudu.
C) He must do tayammum for wudu only.
D) He must find more water before performing tayammum.

Key Takeaway
For Hanafis, the obligation of wudu or ghusl is contingent on finding water sufficient for complete purification; insufficient water triggers tayammum for the entire missing purification without a partial obligation.

Distance for Tayammum: One Mile
Clarification of the condition of distance for tayammum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the distance condition that permits tayammum in the Hanafi school and explain the grammatical parsing of the key phrase.

Core Idea
Tayammum is permitted when water is one mile away – a clear distance condition for the concession.

This lesson examines the grammatical parsing of a phrase in al-Wiqaya that specifies the distance condition for tayammum. Understanding the parsing helps confirm that the distance is measured from the person to the water, and that one mile is the threshold for permissible dry ablution.

Guided Path
1
Locate the phrase and its referents
Identify the phrase 'بعده ميلا' in the source. The pronoun can refer to the person seeking water or to the water itself. Both interpretations lead to the same distance measurement.
The parsing clarifies that the distance is between the person and the water source.
2
Interpret the distance condition
Understand that regardless of which referent is taken, the condition is that water is one mile away. This distance is a concession for performing tayammum.
The distance is approximately 1.6 km or an hour's walk.
3
Apply the ruling
State the Hanafi ruling: If water is at a distance of one mile or more, and one cannot easily fetch it, tayammum is permissible. This is a shart (condition) for the validity of tayammum.
Other conditions for tayammum (e.g., intention, purity of earth) are covered elsewhere.

Source Text

Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a parsing of a phrase in the text of al-Wiqaya. The phrase "بعده ميلا" (due to its distance of a mile) is explained in two possible ways: either the pronoun refers to the people seeking water (i.e., they are one mile away from water), or to the water itself (i.e., the water is one mile away from them). In either case, the ruling is that if water is at a distance of approximately one mile (about 1.6 km or an hour's walk), tayammum is permissible. This distance is a condition for the permissibility of tayammum in the Hanafi school. The commentary clarifies the grammatical attachment to show the intended meaning.

Key Terms
ميل (mīl): A unit of distance approximately one mile (about 1.6 km); the distance that permits tayammum when one is unable to reach the water source.

Tayammum's Specificity: Subsequent Hadath Requires Wudu
Expansion and clarification of a Hanafi ruling on tayammum and subsequent hadath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that tayammum is specific to its cause and state when wudu becomes obligatory after tayammum.

Core Idea
Tayammum only removes the specific hadath it was performed for; a new hadath requires wudu despite valid tayammum.

This passage clarifies a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh: a person in janabah performs tayammum due to water scarcity, but later a minor hadath occurs and water becomes available. Does the existing tayammum suffice, or must he perform wudu? The Hanafi school, through a close reading of the text, establishes that tayammum is cause-specific, so wudu becomes obligatory. We'll explore the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two cases: (1) a junub finds water before tayammum – wudu not obligatory; (2) a junub performs tayammum, then a minor hadath occurs and water is found – wudu becomes obligatory. Recognize the timeline and the type of hadath.
Scenarios differ by timing of water and type of impurity.
2
Interpret the key term 'ma'a'
The word 'ma'a' (مع) is interpreted as 'after' (بعد) to resolve the apparent contradiction. Understand that the author uses this linguistic reinterpretation to show sequence.
This is a common Hanafi method to harmonize conflicting textual indications.
3
Understand the causal link
The letter 'fa' (ف) is causal: it explains why wudu is obligatory – because the prior tayammum was only for janabah, not for the new minor hadath. Grasp that the obligation stems from the new cause.
The causal link refutes the objection that tayammum and wudu cannot coexist.
4
Apply the principle of cause-specificity
Tayammum is specific to its cause (janabah). It does not automatically cover future events of a different type. This is the core legal principle.
This distinguishes Hanafi view from schools that consider tayammum as removing all hadath until water is found.
5
Summarize the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position: wudu is wajib upon finding water after a new minor hadath, despite the continued validity of tayammum for janabah.
This ruling emphasizes that tayammum does not 'merge' with later obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد تفرق الناظرون لإصلاح الكلام إلى مسالك، وقرروا تقريرات أكثرها مخدوشة كما أوضحناه في «السعاية» (١) ، والقولُ المعتمد في هذا المقام أن كلمة : «مع» بمعنى: «بعد»، والمضاف محذوف، أو هي بمعناها ، والمضاف محذوف، وهو التيمم. وحاصله : أنه إذا وجدَ الجُنُب قدراً كافياً للوضوء قبل التيمم لا يجب عليه الوضوء عندنا، وأما إذا تيمم بالجنابة ، ثمَّ طرأ عليه ناقض الوضوء، ووجد ماء كافياً له، يجب عليه الوضوء عندنا أيضاً ، فإنّ التيمم السابق للجنابة لا يرتفع به الحدث الطارئ. وبه ظهر أنَّ «الـفـاء تعليليَّة، والمقصود به رفع ما يقال من أنه كيف يجب عليه الوضوء مع بقاء التيمم، بأنَّ التيمم السابق إنّما هو للجنابة لا للحدث الذي حدث بعده، وقوله: «بالاتفاق» متعلّق بوجوب الوضوء، أو بكون التيمم للجنابة اتفاقا ، وإنما وجب الوضوء للحدث الطارئ ، ومَن اختار أنّ معناه التيمم للجنابة واجب بعد الوضوء فقد أخطأ.
The observers have diverged in paths to rectify the speech, and they established determinations, most of which are defective, as we clarified in 'al-Si'ayah'. The relied-upon statement in this context is that the word 'ma'a' means 'after', and the genitive construct is omitted, or it is with its meaning, and the genitive construct is omitted, and it is tayammum. Its summary: If a junub finds enough water for wudu before tayammum, wudu is not obligatory upon him according to us. But if he performs tayammum due to janabah, then a nullifier of wudu occurs upon him, and he finds enough water for it, wudu becomes obligatory upon him according to us as well. Indeed, the previous tayammum for janabah does not remove the subsequent hadath. By this it becomes apparent that the letter 'fa' is for causation, and the intended meaning is to refute what is said: How can wudu be obligatory while tayammum remains? Because the previous tayammum is only for janabah, not for the hadath that occurred afterward. His statement: 'by agreement' is related to the obligation of wudu, or to the fact that tayammum is for janabah by agreement. Wudu is only obligatory for the subsequent hadath. Whoever chooses that its meaning is that tayammum for janabah is obligatory after wudu has erred.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This is a commentary expanding on a ruling concerning the sequence of tayammum and wudu. The author clarifies that the word 'ma'a' in the original text means 'after', and the understood object is tayammum. The correct position in the Hanafi school is: if a person in a state of major ritual impurity (junub) finds sufficient water for wudu before performing tayammum, then wudu is not obligatory upon him (because he can use the water for ghusl, which is the required purification from janabah). However, if he first performs tayammum due to lack of water (for janabah), and then afterwards a minor hadath (e.g., passing wind) occurs, and at that time he finds water sufficient for wudu, then wudu becomes obligatory upon him. This is because the prior tayammum only addressed the janabah, not the new minor hadath. The 'fa' in the text is causal, explaining why wudu is obligatory despite the existing tayammum. The author refutes a mistaken understanding that tayammum and wudu are incompatible. The authority weight is from the commentary tradition, likely referencing Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
مع (ma'a): In this context, 'ma'a' is interpreted to mean 'after' (بعد), indicating sequence rather than simultaneity.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with clean earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Wudu is not obligatory because tayammum is still valid.
B) Wudu is obligatory because tayammum for janabah does not cover the new minor hadath.
C) Ghusl is required instead of wudu.
D) No purification is needed.

Key Takeaway
Tayammum is cause-specific: it only removes the impurity it was performed for; a new, different hadath requires a new purification.

Defining the Mile for Tayammum: Resolving the Cubit Variation
Defining the distance 'mile' for tayammum permissibility with two opinions resolved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a mile (mīl) and a farsakh, identify the mufta bihi measurement, and apply it to determine the permissibility of tayammum.

Core Idea
Tayammum is permitted when water is one mile away; the mile is 4000 cubits of 24 fingers in the famous Hanafi opinion.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies the distance that permits tayammum: a mile. He defines the mile and the farsakh, and resolves an apparent discrepancy between two opinions by showing that the cubit size adjusts. Understanding this definition is crucial for correctly applying the ruling on tayammum when water is scarce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operative phrase
The phrase 'due to its distance of a mile' from the matn is the trigger. Determine what 'a mile' means in Hanafi law.
2
Parse the term 'mile'
A mile is one-third of a farsakh. The farsakh is 3 miles. The mile is measured in cubits and fingers.
3
Distinguish the two opinions
One opinion: 4000 cubits of 24 fingers each. Another: 3000 cubits of 32 fingers each. Note that the cubit size differs between them.
The difference does not change the actual distance because the cubit size compensates.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The famous opinion (mufta bihi) is 4000 cubits of 24-finger cubit, relied upon by Ibn Nujaym, al-Zayla'i, and al-Saruji.
This is the operative definition for fatwa.
5
Apply the definition
If water is a mile (approx. 1855 m) or more away, tayammum is permissible. If closer, water must be sought.
This applies to all states (hadath, janabah, etc.)

They differed on the measure of the mile according to opinions: three miles by agreement. The famous opinion relied upon by Ibn Nujaym, al-Zayla'i, al-Saruji and others is that it is four thousand cubits, each cubit twenty-four fingers, and the measure of a finger is six barley grains placed belly to back, and the measure of each barley grain is six horse hairs from the tail of a Turkish horse. This agrees with the opinion of later arithmeticians. The second opinion: it is three thousand cubits, based on taking the cubit as thirty-two fingers according to the opinion of ancient arithmeticians. The difference between these two opinions does not amount to much, because it does not cause a difference in the distance of the mile varying between the two cubits. (4) In al-Bahr al-Ra'iq (1:146), but he mentioned that what is meant here is one-third of a farsakh, and the farsakh is twelve thousand paces, each pace one and a half cubits of the common cubit, and it is twenty-four fingers. This is in al-Yanabi'. (5) In Tabyin al-Haqa'iq (1:37). (6) He is Ahmad ibn Ibrahim ibn Abd al-Ghani al-Saruji... (7) Like the author of al-Binayah (1:482), and al-Hidayah al-Ala'iyyah (p.34), and al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:155). (8) The cubit equals 46.375 cm. See: introduction of Majma' al-Bahrayn (p.48). (9) The finger equals 1.932 cm. See: introduction of Majma' al-Bahrayn (p.48).

Commentary
This passage defines the distance that permits tayammum: a mile. The author discusses the meaning of the phrase 'due to its distance of a mile' from the original matn, explaining that it refers to the distance of the person (whether hadath, junub, etc.) from water, or the distance of water from them. He then provides the definition of a mile and a farsakh. The mile is one-third of a farsakh. There is a famous opinion that a mile is 4000 cubits, each cubit being 24 fingers. The finger is defined as 6 barley grains, and each barley grain as 6 horse hairs. A second opinion says a mile is 3000 cubits, but the author notes this difference is not practically significant. The passage also includes hadith evidence for the permissibility of tayammum at a mile's distance.

ميل (mīl): A unit of distance used for the permissibility of tayammum; defined as one-third of a farsakh, approximately 4000 cubits.
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance equal to three miles; from the root meaning 'spaciousness'.
ذراع (dhirā'): A cubit; 24 fingers in the famous opinion, approximately 46.375 cm.

The Hanafi Mile and Farsakh: Definitions and Disputes
Definition and expansion on mile and farsakh measurements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi definition of the mile and farsakh, identify the four opinions within the school, and apply the measurement to determine travel distances for qasr and tayammum.

Core Idea
The mile is 4000 cubits of 24 fingers each — the mufta bihi measure for travel distances in Hanafi fiqh.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the definition of the mile (mil) and farsakh, essential for determining distances for rulings like shortening prayer and tayammum. The author presents four opinions within the Hanafi school and identifies the relied-upon position. This lesson unpacks the measurement system and the rationale for the preferred view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic relationship
Note that the mile is defined as one-third of a farsakh. All opinions agree on this ratio.
The farsakh is consistently three miles.
2
Understand the measurement chain
The relied-upon opinion uses a chain: 1 mile = 4000 cubits; 1 cubit = 24 fingers; 1 finger = 6 barley grains (belly-to-back); 1 barley grain = 6 horse hairs from a Turkish horse's tail.
This chain ensures consistency across measurements.
3
Recognize the four opinions
List them: (1) 4000 cubits (24-finger cubit) — relied upon; (2) 3000 cubits (32-finger cubit); (3) 4000 paces (1.5 cubit pace) — rejected; (4) range 3000–4000 cubits (attributed to Ibn Shuja').
Opinions 1 and 2 are equivalent in distance due to different cubit standards.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The well-known (mashhur) opinion is the first, affirmed by Ibn Nujaym, al-Zayla'i, al-Saruji, and others. The third opinion is explicitly rejected.
The fourth opinion is merely a reference to the early-late arithmetician dispute.
5
Apply the measure in fiqh
Use the relied-upon measure: for qasr, the distance is 3 farsakhs = 9 miles = approximately 36,000 cubits (~ 16.7 km).
This is the operative distance for shortening prayer and permissibility of tayammum for a traveler.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الميل ثلث الفرسخ (۱۱) ، وقيل : ثلاثة آلاف ذراع وخمسمئة إلى أربعة آلاف ، وما ذُكِرَ ظاهرُ الرُّواية وهو

قوله : الفرسخ (۲) ؛ الفرسخة في اللغة بمعنى : السعة، ومنه اشتق الفرسخ، واختلفوا في مقدار الميل على أقوال : ثلاثة أميال اتفاقاً،

فالمشهور الذي اعتمد عليه ابن نجيم والزَّيْلَعِي والسروجي وغيرهم أنه أربعة الآف ذراع، كل ذراع أربعة وعشرون أصبعاً، ومقدار الإصبع ست شعيرات مضمومة البطون إلى الظهور، ومقدار كلِّ شعيرة ست شعور من ذنب الفرس التركي، وهذا موافق لرأي المتأخرين من الحساب.

والقول الثاني : إنه ثلاثة الآف ذراع، وهو مبني على أخذ الذراع بقدر اثنين وثلاثين إصبعاً على ما هو رأي قدماء الحساب.

والخلاف بين هذين القولين لا يرجع إلى طائل، فإنّه لا يورث اختلافاً في مقدار مسافة الميل المتفاوت بين الذراعين.

والثالث : أنه أربعةُ الآفِ خطوة كل خطوة ذراع ونصف بذراع العامة، وهو أربعة وعشرون إصبعاً ، فتكون ذراع الميل سنة الآف، وهو قول لا يعتمد عليه، صرح به الخيرُ الرملي وغيره.

والرابع : ما ذكره الشارح بلفظ : قيل : وذكره في «الذخيرة» منسوباً إلى ابن شجاع ثلاثة الآف ذراع وخمسئة إلى أربعة الآف، ولعله إشارة إلى الخلاف الواقع بين القدماء والمتأخرين من أهل الحساب، وإن شئت مزيد تفصيل في هذا المبحث، فارجع إلى رسالتي : «الإفادة الخطيرة المتعلقة ببحث سبع عرض شعيرة) من شرح ملخص الخميني في علم الهيأة».
The mile is one-third of the farsakh, and it was said: three thousand cubits and five hundred to four thousand. And what was mentioned is the apparent of the narration. He said: The farsakh; al-farsakhah in language means spaciousness, from which farsakh is derived. They differed regarding the amount of the mile according to statements: three miles by agreement. So the well-known [opinion] relied upon by Ibn Nujaym, al-Zayla'i, al-Saruji and others is that it is four thousand cubits, each cubit being twenty-four fingers, and the measure of a finger is six barley grains placed belly-to-back, and the measure of each barley grain is six horse hairs from the tail of a Turkish horse, and this agrees with the view of the later [scholars] of arithmetic. The second statement: that it is three thousand cubits, built upon taking the cubit as thirty-two fingers according to the view of the early [scholars] of arithmetic. The difference between these two statements is of no substantial consequence, for it does not cause a difference in the measure of the mile distance that varies between the two cubits. The third: that it is four thousand paces, each pace being a cubit and a half by the common cubit, which is twenty-four fingers, so the cubit of the mile becomes six thousand, and this is a statement not relied upon, explicitly stated by al-Khayr al-Ramli and others. The fourth: what the commentator mentioned with the wording 'it was said', and mentioned in al-Dhakhirah attributed to Ibn Shuja': three thousand cubits and five hundred to four thousand, and perhaps it is a reference to the disagreement between the early and later [scholars] of arithmetic. And if you desire more detail on this topic, refer to my treatise: 'Al-Ifada al-Khatirah' related to the topic of the seven barley grains from the commentary on Mukhtasar al-Khazini on astronomy.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a definition and expansion on the legal measurements of the mile and farsakh, specifically in the context of defining distances for rulings such as shortening prayer and tayammum. The author begins by stating the matn: 'The mile is one-third of a farsakh, and it was said: three thousand cubits and five hundred to four thousand.' Then he comments on the word 'farsakh', noting its linguistic root meaning spaciousness, and states that there are multiple opinions on the exact length of a mile, though all agree it is three miles. The relied-upon (mu'tamad) position, as held by later scholars like Ibn Nujaym and al-Zayla'i, is that a mile equals 4000 cubits, each cubit being 24 fingers, each finger 6 barley grains (belly-to-back), each barley grain 6 horse hairs. This is the view of the later arithmeticians and is the operative ruling for fatwa. There is a second opinion of 3000 cubits using a larger cubit of 32 fingers, but the author notes this difference is inconsequential for practical fiqh as the physical distance remains the same due to the different cubit lengths. A third opinion of 4000 paces (each pace 1.5 common cubits) is explicitly rejected as unreliable. A fourth opinion from al-Dhakhirah gives a range of 3000-4000 cubits, possibly reflecting the early-late arithmetician dispute. The author directs readers to his own treatise for further detail.

Key Terms
ميل (mīl): A unit of distance equal to one-third of a farsakh (about 1855 meters according to the relied-upon opinion).
فرسخ (farsakh): A unit of distance of approximately 3 miles (about 5.565 km). The word derives from a root meaning 'spaciousness'.
ذراع (dhirāʿ): A cubit, used as a unit of length. In this passage, it is defined as either 24 or 32 fingers depending on the opinion.
أصبع (uṣbuʿ): A finger's breadth, a unit of length equal to 6 barley grains placed belly-to-back.
شعيرة (shuʿayrah): A barley grain, used as a standard unit of measurement for small lengths.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in this passage, what is the length of one mile?
A) 3000 cubits (32-finger cubit)
B) 4000 cubits (24-finger cubit)
C) 4000 paces (1.5 cubit pace)
D) 3000 to 4000 cubits range

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi mile is 4000 cubits of 24 fingers each, and the intra-school dispute is practically inconsequential.

Conditions Permitting Tayammum: Distance, Illness, and Other Excuses
Explaining excuses for tayammum with detailed distance discussion and illness dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for tayammum related to distance and illness, and explain the rational proof for the illness concession contra Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Tayammum is permitted when water is unavailable or harmful; the Hanafi school extends the harm excuse to mere worsening of illness, unlike Shafi'i.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the various excuses that make tayammum permissible in the Hanafi school. It begins with a technical discussion of the mile (distance) and then addresses illness, cold, enemy, and thirst. The main inter-madhhab dispute concerns illness: the Hanafis do not require fear of death, unlike the Shafi'is, using a qiyas from financial hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
Tayammum is permissible when water is absent or harmful. The excuses are distance, illness, cold, enemy, and thirst.
Each excuse has its own conditions.
2
Analyze the distance condition
The mile (mil) is the threshold distance. The text presents several opinions on its length: 4000 steps (unreliable), 3500-4000 cubits (relied upon), and half-hour walk. When water is in front, two miles are needed; otherwise one mile suffices.
The relied-upon opinion is from later scholars like Fath Bab al-Inayah.
3
Examine the illness excuse
The Hanafi rule: tayammum is permitted if using water would worsen illness, even without fear of death. The Shafi'i requires fear of death or limb loss.
The text explicitly states 'contrary to al-Shafi'i'.
4
Understand the rational proof (dalil aqli)
The Hanafi proof is qiyas: financial hardship (paying excessive price for water) permits tayammum by consensus; bodily harm is more severe, so it should also permit tayammum.
This is an argument from a fortiori (awla).
5
Review the remaining excuses
Cold: using water would harm. Enemy: fear of harm from enemy. Thirst: fear of thirst if water is used. Mere fear suffices, not actual presence.
These are not disputed with Shafi'i.

Among those who relied on this opinion are: the author of al-Maraqi, Fath Bab al-Inayah, Ibn Malik in his commentary on al-Wiqayah, the author of al-Bahr, and al-Inayah. He is Khayr al-Din ibn Ahmad ibn Ali al-Ayyubi al-Ulaymi al-Faruqi al-Ramli al-Hanafi: said al-Muhibbi: the imam, jurist, hadith scholar, exegete, linguist, morphologist, grammarian, rhetorician, prosodist, the senior scholar of the Hanafis in his time, author of famous fatwas. Among his works: al-Fatawa al-Khayriyya, Hawaashi on Manh al-Ghaffar, Hawaashi on Sharh al-Kanz of al-Ayni, and Hawaashi on al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair. (993-1081 AH). He is Muhammad ibn Shuja' al-Thalji, Abu Abdullah, attributed to Thalj ibn Amr ibn Malik ibn Abd Manaf; he is not attributed to selling ice, and is called Ibn al-Thalji. He was the jurist of Iraq in his time, prominent in fiqh and hadith with piety and worship. Among his works: Tashih al-Athar, al-Nawadir, al-Mudarabah, al-Radd ala al-Mushabbihah, and al-Manasik in over sixty parts. (d. 266 AH). Among his works: Risalah fi al-Hisab, Quwwat al-Kawakib wa Du'fuha, and Sharh Tariq fi Masa'il al-Wasaya (d. ca. 618 AH). Or illness, or cold, or enemy, or thirst. 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah. [1] According to the narration of al-Hasan: The mile is only considered if it is in a direction other than in front of him, so that it becomes two miles going and returning; but if it is in front of him, it is considered to be two miles. (Or due to illness) that prevents him from being able to use water, and if he uses water his illness would worsen: fear of death is not a condition, contrary to al-Shafi'i; for the harm of worsening the illness is greater than the harm of increased price, and that permits tayammum. (Or cold) such that using water harms him. [4] (Or enemy or thirst): meaning if he fears thirst upon using water. 1] His statement: 'in a direction other than in front of him': meaning that the water is from the direction of behind, right, or left, so that if the one performing wudu goes to it, it becomes one mile returning and one mile going. [2] His statement: 'or due to illness': connected to his statement: 'distance of a mile', and the reason for repeating the conjunction here and not after it is that the excuses of unavailability and illness are explicitly mentioned in the Quran, so it is appropriate to give them independent mention and make the rest following. [3] His statement: 'until... etc.': meaning that fear of worsening illness and the like that causes hardship is sufficient to permit tayammum without the condition of fear of destruction of life or limb; because the harm of worsening, even if it does not reach destruction, is stronger than the harm of increased price of water; it being apparent that bodily harm is more severe than financial harm. And the harm of increased price permits it by consensus: if he does not find water except at a price, then if its price is equal to or less than the fair price, he buys it and performs wudu with it; but if its price is higher, he is not obligated to buy it, rather tayammum is permitted by consensus. [4] His statement: 'i.e... etc.': he indicated by it that it is not the mere existence of thirst that permits, but rather fear of thirst, whether he has water or not, and he fears thirst if he uses water, then he performs tayammum.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains the conditions that permit tayammum (dry ablution). It opens with a detailed discussion of the distance (mil) that qualifies as being far from water. The author presents multiple opinions on the exact length of a mile: one opinion is four thousand steps (each step 1.5 common cubits = 24 fingers, making a cubit of the mile six thousand), but this is said to be unreliable. Another attributed to Ibn Shuja' is between 3,500 and 4,000 cubits. The commentary then cites scholarly works that rely on these views and provides biographical notes on some scholars. The core legal part begins with the matn phrase 'Or illness, or cold, or enemy, or thirst.' The commentary explains each: For illness, the condition is that one cannot use water or using it would worsen the illness. Importantly, the Hanafi school does not require fear of death from using water—unlike the Shafi'i school—because the harm of worsening illness outweighs the financial harm of buying expensive water, and financial harm already permits tayammun by consensus. For cold, use of water must cause harm. For enemy and thirst, the mere fear of thirst—whether one has water or not—is sufficient. The commentary also clarifies that the distance rule only applies when water is behind or to the side; if it is in front, the distance required is two miles (one going, one returning). This passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of using rational analogy (qiyas) and preference (istihsan) to extend the ruling beyond the explicit Quranic allowance for illness and unavailability.

Key Terms
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with clean earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
ميل (mil): A unit of distance, approximately 4000 cubits or 2 km, varying by opinion.
مرض (marad): Illness that prevents using water or is worsened by it.
ضرر (darar): Harm or injury, used in the qiyas argument.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi position on the length of the mile (distance) for tayammum?
A) Exactly 4000 cubits
B) 4000 steps (one step = 1.5 cubits)
C) Between 3500 and 4000 cubits, based on later scholars
D) Half an hour's walk, according to all authorities

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits tayammum not only when water is absent but also when using it would cause harm, including worsening illness without fear of death, based on qiyas from financial hardship.

Taharah — Purification

Excuses for Tayammum: The Hanafi Position
Listing excuses for tayammum with conditions and a cross-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five excuses that permit tayammum in the Hanafi school and explain why fear of intensified illness suffices without requiring fear of death, using the Hanafi proof from harm hierarchy.

Core Idea
Tayammum is permitted for illness when water worsens it—no death required, because bodily harm outweighs financial harm.

In this lesson, we examine the excuses that allow a Muslim to perform tayammum instead of wudu or ghusl when water is harmful or unavailable. The Hanafi school lists five specific excuses, and for illness they take a broader view than the Shafi'is: mere intensification of illness is enough, even if death is not feared. We will see how the Hanafi proof (dalil) uses a qiyas from financial harm to bodily harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five excuses
List the excuses mentioned: illness (marad), cold (bard), enemy (aduw), thirst (atash), and lack of tools (adamm al-ala).
These are cumulative; any one suffices.
2
Understand the condition for each excuse
For illness: using water would intensify the illness. For cold: water harms due to extreme cold. For enemy/thirst: fear of harm from using water (e.g., enemy attack, future thirst). For lack of tools: inability to draw water without equipment.
The common thread is harm or inability.
3
Analyze the Hanafi position on illness
The Hanafi school does not require fear of death (talf); mere intensification is sufficient. This differs from Imam al-Shafi'i who requires fear of death or loss of limb.
The text explicitly states 'لا يشترط خوف التلف خلافا للشافعي'.
4
Examine the Hanafi proof
The proof is a qiyas: financial harm (e.g., increased price of water) permits tayammum by consensus; bodily harm (intensified illness) is greater than financial harm. Therefore, bodily harm a fortiori permits tayammum.
The reasoning uses 'اولوية' (a fortiori) argument.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
A person with a cold who fears it worsening may perform tayammum in the Hanafi school, while Shafi'is would require him to use water unless death is feared.
This highlights the real-world impact of the ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي رواية الحسن بن زياد: الميل إنما يكون معتبراً إذا كان في طرف غير قدامه، حتى يصير ميلين ذهابا ومجيئاً، وأما إذا كان في قدامه فيعتبر أن يكون ميلين. (أو لمرض) لا يقدر معه على استعمال الماء، وإن استعمل الماء اشتد مرضه لا يشترط خوف التلف خلافا للشافعي؛ إذ ضرر اشتداد المرض فوق ضرر زيادة الثمن، وهو يبيح التيمم. (أو برد) إن استعمل الماء يضره. (أو عدو أو عطش) أي إن استعمل الماء خاف العطش. وأبيح الماء للشرب حتى إذا وجد المسافر ماء في حب معدا للشرب جاز له التيمم إلا إذا كان كثيرا. فيستدل على أنه للشرب والوضوء، فأما الماء المعد للوضوء فإنه يجوز أن يشرب منه، وعند الإمام الفضل بن عيسى: عكس هذا، فلا يجوز التيمم. (أو عدم آلة) كالدلو ونحوه.
And in the narration of Hasan ibn Ziyad: The mile is considered only if it is on a side other than in front of him, so that it becomes two miles going and coming. But if it is in front of him, then it is considered to be two miles. (Or due to illness) that he cannot use water, and if he uses water his illness intensifies. Fear of death is not required, contrary to al-Shafi'i, because the harm of intensified illness is above the harm of increased price, and that permits tayammum. (Or cold) if he uses water it harms him. (Or enemy or thirst): i.e., if he uses water he fears thirst. And water is made permissible for drinking such that if a traveler finds water in a jar prepared for drinking, tayammum is permissible for him unless it is abundant. So it is inferred that it is for drinking and ablution. As for water prepared for ablution, it is permissible to drink from it. And according to Imam al-Fadl ibn Isa: the opposite of this, so tayammum is not permissible. (Or lack of tool) like a bucket and the like.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions that permit tayammum (dry ablution) as an alternative to wudu when water is unavailable or harmful. The author begins with a note from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad about the calculation of distance (mile) for travel allowance—a point relevant to determining when one qualifies as a traveler. The main content lists five excuses: illness, cold, enemy, thirst, and lack of tools. For illness, the key point is that the person cannot use water or its use would worsen the condition. Importantly, the Hanafi school does not require fear of death (talf); mere harm is sufficient, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i. The reasoning is that bodily harm outweighs financial harm (increase in price), and financial harm already permits tayammum. For cold, the harm is clear. For enemy and thirst, the person fears harm from using water. A special case is discussed: when water is set aside for drinking (e.g., in a jar), if it is abundant it cannot be used for wudu; if scarce, tayammum is permitted. The opposite view of Imam al-Fadl ibn Isa is noted, where water for wudu can be used for drinking but tayammum is not allowed. Finally, lack of tools (like a bucket) to draw water excuses one from using it.

Key Terms
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with clean earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful
المرض (al-marad): Illness that prevents using water or makes it harmful, even if death is not feared
العدو (al-aduw): Enemy or threat from a person, animal, or natural element that makes using water risky

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi proof for not requiring fear of death for tayammum due to illness?
A) Direct Quranic verse: 'and do not kill yourselves'
B) Qiyas (analogy) from financial harm: if increased price permits tayammum, then bodily harm permits it a fortiori
C) Hadith: 'The Prophet did tayammum for a wound'
D) Consensus of the companions

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, tayammum is permitted for illness even without fear of death, based on the principle that bodily harm outweighs financial harm.

Tayammum Due to Fear of Thirst or Lack of Water Vessel
Ruling and conditions for tayammum when water is scarce or vessel lacking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for tayammum when water is present but allocated for drinking or vessel is lacking, and explain the intra-Hanafi difference regarding water placed for drinking.

Core Idea
Tayammum is wajib when using water for wudu would cause thirst, even if water is present but allocated for drinking.

The general rule in Hanafi fiqh is that tayammum is only allowed when water is absent or harmful. However, this passage introduces a critical exception: when water is present but using it for wudu would cause thirst (for oneself or others), tayammum becomes obligatory. The text also addresses the scenario of lacking a vessel (e.g., bucket) and the allocation of water for drinking, along with an intra-Hanafi disagreement on the latter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? (Tayammum) What is its hukm level in this scenario? The passage states 'jaza' (permissible) but the commentary clarifies it is used in a sense that includes obligation (wajib).
The commentary explicitly says 'al-jawaz huna musta'mal fima ya'ummu al-wujub'.
2
Locate the conditions for permissibility
List the conditions mentioned: (a) fear of thirst if wudu is performed with the water, (b) lack of a vessel (e.g., bucket) to draw water, (c) water allocated for drinking. The passage also extends the fear to companions and even a permissible dog.
The condition 'fear of thirst' applies whether thirst is actually present or not.
3
Analyze the dalil (proof)
The ruling is based on qiyas (analogy): the necessity of preserving life (thirst) takes precedence over using water for wudu, analogous to a traveler with limited water. The proof is not directly quoted but inferred.
Dalil type: qiyas, zanni in both thubut and dalala.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam al-Fadli holds that if water is placed for drinking, one must use it for wudu and tayammum is not allowed. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the opposite: tayammum is permissible because the water is tied to the right of drinkers.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its authority
The standard Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah: tayammum is wajib when fearing thirst, even if water is available but allocated for drinking. Imam al-Fadli's view is considered a nawadir opinion.
Authority weight: zahir_riwayah for the main position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عدم آلة : كالدلو، ونحوه. خاف العطش إن توضأ بالماء يجوز له التيمم سواءً عرض له العطش أم لا، سواء خافه على نفسه أو على رفيقه أعم من أن يكون مخالطاً له ، أو آخر من معه في القافلة، أو على كليه أو كلب رفيقه إذا كان مباحَ الاقتناء ككلب الصيد. كذا في «الدر المختار» وحواشيه (1) [۱] قوله : أو أبيح ؛ ظاهره مختل، واختلف الناظرون في توجيهه، فقيل: إنه عطف من جهة المعنى ؛ أي إذا خاف العطش من جهة استعمال الماء، وأبيح الماء للشرب، وقيل: هو عطف على عطش، بتأويل الجملة بالمفرد ؛ أي ولإباحة الماء للشرب. (۲) قوله : حُبّ معداً ... الخ بضم الحاء المهمة، وتشديد الباء : الجرة العظيمة والخابية فارسية خم، وفي أكثر النسخ بالجيم : وهو البئر التي لم تطو. (۳) قوله : جاز له التيمم ؛ الجواز هاهنا مستعمل فيما يعم الوجوب، فإن التيمم في مثل هذه الصورة واجب، والتقييد بالمسافرِ اتفاقي ؛ لأن وجود مثل هذه الصورة وهو أن لا يجد إلا ماءً موضوعاً في خابية معد للشرب نادر. [٤]قوله : فيستدل ؛ أي يعلمُ بكون الماء المهيأ للواردين كثيراً على أنه أبيح للشرب والوضوء ونحو ذلك، فيجوز به الوضوء، ولا يجوز التيمم ؛ لقدرته على ما يتوضأ به ، بخلاف ما إذا كان مباحاً للشرب فقط، فإنه حينئذ لم يقدر على ماء فارغ عن الحوائج الضرورية ؛ لتعلق حق الشاربين به، وخوف عطش الواردين إن استعمله للوضوء. [0]قوله : وعند... الخ ؛ قال في «الذخيرة» : كان الشيخ أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل يقول : الماء الموضوع لشرب الناس إذا توضأ به رجل حلّ له ذلك، ولو كان وضع ليتوضأ الناسُ به لا يحل لأحد أن يشرب منه، فعلى قياس قوله، إذا وجد ماء وضع لشرب الناس لا يجوز له التيمم.
Or lack of a vessel: such as a bucket and the like. He fears thirst if he performs wudu with water; tayammum is permissible for him, whether thirst is present or not, whether he fears it for himself or his companion, whether the companion is mixing with him or another from the caravan, or for both, or for his companion's dog if it is permissible to keep, like a hunting dog. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its glosses. [1] His saying: 'or water is permitted for drinking' – its apparent meaning is problematic, and the viewers differed in its interpretation. It is said: it is a conjunctive from the perspective of meaning: i.e., if he fears thirst from using the water, and water is permitted for drinking. And it is said: it is a conjunctive upon 'thirst', by interpreting the sentence as a single word: i.e., and for the permissibility of water for drinking. (2) His saying: 'a jar prepared' etc. – with damma on the ha and tashdid on the ba: a large jar, and khābiya is Persian for a jar, and in most copies with jim: it is a well that has not been lined. (3) His saying: 'tayammum is permissible for him' – permissibility here is used in a sense that includes obligation, for tayammum in such a scenario is obligatory, and the restriction to the traveler is coincidental, because the occurrence of such a scenario—that he finds only water placed in a jar prepared for drinking—is rare. [4] His saying: 'so it is inferred' – i.e., it is known that the water prepared for arrivals is abundant in that it is permitted for drinking and wudu and the like, so wudu with it is permissible, and tayammum is not permissible, because he is able to perform wudu. Contrary to if it is permitted for drinking only, for then he is not able to use water free from necessary needs, due to the right of the drinkers attached to it, and fear of thirst of the arrivals if he uses it for wudu. [0] His saying: 'and according to...' etc. – he said in al-Dhakhira: Shaykh Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl used to say: water placed for people to drink—if a man performs wudu with it, that is permissible for him. And if it was placed for people to perform wudu, it is not permissible for anyone to drink from it. So by analogy with his statement, if he finds water placed for people to drink, tayammum is not permissible for him.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on tayammum due to fear of thirst or lack of a water vessel. The main text states that if one fears thirst if he uses water for wudu, tayammum is permissible. This applies whether thirst is actually present or not, and whether the fear is for oneself, one's companion, or even a permissible dog. The commentary expands on the phrase 'or water is permitted for drinking', explaining that it refers to a scenario where water is allocated for drinking, and if the water is abundant, it may be used for both drinking and wudu, so tayammum is not allowed. However, if the water is only for drinking and fear of thirst exists, tayammum is permitted. An intra-Hanafi difference is mentioned: Imam al-Fadli holds the opposite view regarding water placed for drinking—he says if water is for drinking, it is permissible to use it for wudu and tayammum is not allowed. But the relied-upon position is that tayammum is permissible in such cases due to the right of the drinkers.

Key Terms
التيمم (al-tayammum): Dry ablution with clean earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or its use is harmful.
الآلة (al-ala): Vessel or tool for drawing water, such as a bucket.
الحب (al-hubb): A large jar or earthenware vessel for storing water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when is tayammum permissible due to lack of a water vessel?
A) Only if one has no water at all.
B) If one has water but no vessel to draw it, and fears thirst.
C) If one has a vessel but no water.
D) Only if one is a traveler.

Key Takeaway
Tayammum is wajib when using water for wudu would cause thirst or when a necessary vessel is missing, even if water is present but allocated for drinking.

Tayammum Due to Fear of Missing 'Eid Prayer
Ruling on tayammum for fear of missing 'Eid prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition under which tayammum is permissible for 'Eid prayer, explain the underlying rationale (no makeup), and identify the exception when multiple congregations are available.

Core Idea
Tayammum is permitted for time-bound prayers with no makeup, even if water is present.

We now explore a unique excuse for tayammum: fear of missing the 'Eid prayer. Unlike regular prayers, 'Eid has no make-up (qada'), so its time is critical. The Hanafi school permits tayammum in this case, even if water is available, provided there is no alternative congregation nearby. This ruling is supported by the Prophet's practice and analogy to the funeral prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is performing tayammum instead of wudu for an 'Eid prayer. The ruling: it is wajib (obligatory) to perform tayammum if using water would cause missing the 'Eid prayer, because the 'Eid prayer itself is wajib according to the Hanafi school.
2
Locate the condition for this ruling
The condition is 'fear of missing the 'Eid prayer at its beginning' – meaning the time will lapse (sunset or imam finishes) before wudu can be completed. This fear must be reasonable.
The text states: 'if he fears missing the 'Eid prayer if he performs wudu'.
3
Understand the rationale (illah)
The illah (effective cause) is that the 'Eid prayer has no make-up (la qada'a laha). Therefore, the ability to use water is considered like inability, because missing the prayer entirely is a greater harm.
The passage says: 'the 'Eid prayer is lost without recourse; since it has no make-up, the ability to use water is like inability.'
4
Examine the textual evidence (dalil)
The author cites the hadith where the Prophet performed tayammum in Medina to respond to a greeting, even though he could have used water later. This proves tayammum is allowed for any matter that cannot be delayed. By analogy (qiyas), 'Eid prayer, which has no makeup, is even more deserving.
The dalil is also supported by athar (traditions) from Companions allowing tayammum for funeral prayer due to fear of missing it.
5
Note the exception (istithna)
If there are multiple 'Eid prayer congregations in the locality, one should not do tayammum but instead perform wudu and go to another mosque where the prayer is still ongoing or about to start.
The text: 'if there are multiple 'Eid prayers in a locality and he fears missing it with the imam in a specific mosque, he does not perform tayammum, but performs wudu and goes to another mosque.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو خوف فوتِ صلاة العيد في الابتداء) : أي إذا خافَ فوت صلاة العيد يقول : الماء الموضوع لشرب الناس إذا توضأ به رجل حلّ له ذلك، ولو كان وضع ليتوضأ الناسُ به لا يحل لأحد أن يشرب منه، فعلى قياس قوله، إذا وجد ماء وضع لشرب الناس لا يجوز له التيمم. [١] قوله : أو خوف ؛ عطف على ما سبق من الأعذار ، وحاصله : أنه إذا خاف فوت صلاة عيد الفطر أو الأضحى لو توضاً يجوز له أن يتيمم ويصلي العيد، وإن كان صحيحاً واجداً للماء قادراً عليه، وذلك بأن يخاف زوال الشمس وذهاب الوقت، أو فراغ الإمام من صلاة العيد. والوجه في ذلك أنّ صلاة العيد تفوتُ لا إلى خلف ؛ إذ لا قضاء لها، فكانت القدرة على الماء كعدم القدرة ، فإن تعددت صلاة العيد في موضع وخاف فوتها مع الإمام في مسجد خاص لا يتيمم ، بل يتوضأ ويذهب إلى مسجد آخر. والأصل فيه ما ورد أنَّ النبي الهلال تيمم في المدينة لجواب السلام ؛ فإنّه سلّم عليه رجل وهو يبول ، فلما فرغ تيمم وردّ عليه السلام ) ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما. وما ورد من الآثارِ من جواز التيمم لصلاة الجنازة عند خوف فوتها، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة والنسائي والطحاوي عن ابن عباس له ، والطحاوي عن الحسن وإبراهيم وعطاء وابن شهاب وغيرهم الله، والبيهقي والدارقطني عن ابن عمر ) ، فان كل
Or fear of missing the 'Eid prayer at its beginning: i.e., if he fears missing the 'Eid prayer, he says: water placed for people to drink—if a man performs wudu with it, that is permissible for him. And if it was placed for people to perform wudu, it is not permissible for anyone to drink from it. So by analogy with his statement, if he finds water placed for people to drink, tayammum is not permissible for him. [1] His saying: 'or fear' – it is a conjunctive upon the previous excuses. Its summary: if he fears missing the prayer of 'Eid al-Fitr or 'Eid al-Adha if he performs wudu, it is permissible for him to perform tayammum and pray 'Eid, even if he is healthy and has water and is able to use it. That is when he fears the sun setting and the time passing, or the imam finishing the 'Eid prayer. The reason is that the 'Eid prayer is lost without recourse; since it has no make-up, the ability to use water is like inability. If there are multiple 'Eid prayers in a locality and he fears missing it with the imam in a specific mosque, he does not perform tayammum, but performs wudu and goes to another mosque. The basis for this is what is related that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم performed tayammum in Medina to respond to the greeting; for a man greeted him while he was urinating, and when he finished, he performed tayammum and returned the greeting. This is narrated by Bukhari and Muslim and others. And what is related of traditions about the permissibility of tayammum for the funeral prayer when fearing it will be missed, narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba, al-Nasa'i, al-Tahawi from Ibn Abbas, and al-Tahawi from al-Hasan, Ibrahim, Ata, Ibn Shihab and others, and al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni from Ibn Umar.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage discusses a further excuse for tayammum: fear of missing the 'Eid prayer. The main text states that if one fears that performing wudu will cause him to miss the 'Eid prayer, he may perform tayammum and pray, even if he is healthy and has water. This is because the 'Eid prayer has no make-up (qada'), so the time is critical. However, if there are multiple 'Eid congregations in the area, he should not do tayammum but go to another mosque after performing wudu. The commentary cites the hadith of the Prophet doing tayammum to respond to a greeting, and athar from the Companions allowing tayammum for funeral prayer due to fear of missing it. The analogy is that both 'Eid and funeral prayers are time-sensitive with no makeup, so tayammum is allowed.

Key Terms
صلاة العيد (salat al-'id): The two 'Eid prayers: 'Eid al-Fitr and 'Eid al-Adha.
القضاء (al-qada): Make-up prayer for a missed obligatory prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because 'Eid prayer is more important than regular prayers.
B) Because 'Eid prayer has no make-up (qada'), so missing it is irreversible.
C) Because tayammum is always preferred for 'Eid.
D) Because water is not actually needed for 'Eid prayer.

Key Takeaway
Tayammum is permitted for time-bound prayers like 'Eid that have no make-up, even with water present, provided no alternative congregation is available.

Tayammum for Fear of Missing Eid Prayer: The Principle of Fawt Lā ilā Khalaf
Ruling with proof on tayammum for Eid prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that tayammum is permitted for fear of missing Eid prayer, derive the underlying principle (fawt lā ilā khalaf), and apply it to similar cases such as funeral prayer.

Core Idea
Tayammum is allowed for acts that cannot be made up, like Eid prayer, even if water is available.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āyah expands on a classic Hanafi ruling: a healthy person with water may still perform tayammum if he fears missing the Eid prayer by using water. The key is that Eid prayer cannot be repeated (qadā'). We will unpack the ruling, its proof, and the underlying legal maxim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What act is being ruled upon? (tayammum for Eid prayer) What is the hukm level? (permissible with a condition). Note the condition: fear of missing the prayer if wudu is performed.
The text says 'jā'iz' (permissible), not obligatory.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Abu al-Juhaym: the Prophet performed tayammum to return a greeting. Also traditions on tayammum for funeral prayer.
This is a Sunnah ahad hadith, but accepted in Hanafi usul as sufficient for practice.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the hadith prove the ruling? The Prophet used tayammum for a non-obligatory act (returning salam) that was time-sensitive. By qiyas, Eid prayer is also time-sensitive and cannot be made up, so tayammum is allowed.
The 'illa (effective cause) is fear of missing a non-repeatable act.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i does not permit tayammum for Eid prayer because in his school, Eid prayer can be made up (qadā'). The dispute hinges on whether Eid prayer is fawt lā ilā khalaf.
The Hanafi position relies on the view that Eid prayer has no make-up.
5
Apply the principle to other cases
The same ruling applies to funeral prayer (also non-repeatable). The text mentions traditions allowing tayammum for funeral prayer due to fear of missing it.
This extends the principle: tayammum for any act that cannot be repeated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو خوف فوت صلاة العيد في الابتداء) : أي إذا خافَ فوت صلاة العيد يقول : الماء الموضوع لشرب الناس إذا توضأ به رجل حلّ له ذلك، ولو كان وضع ليتوضأ الناسُ به لا يحل لأحد أن يشرب منه، فعلى قياس قوله، إذا وجد ماء وضع لشرب الناس لا يجوز له التيمم.
[١] قوله : أو خوف ؛ عطف على ما سبق من الأعذار ، وحاصله : أنه إذا خاف فوت صلاة عيد الفطر أو الأضحى لو توضأ يجوز له أن يتيمم ويصلي العيد، وإن كان صحيحاً واجداً للماء قادراً عليه، وذلك بأن يخاف زوال الشمس وذهاب الوقت، أو فراغ الإمام من صلاة العيد.
والوجه في ذلك أنّ صلاة العيد تفوتُ لا إلى خلف ؛ إذ لا قضاء لها، فكانت القدرة على الماء كعدم القدرة ، فإن تعددت صلاة العيد في موضع وخاف فوتها مع الإمام في مسجد خاص لا يتيمم ، بل يتوضأ ويذهب إلى مسجد آخر. والأصل فيه ما ورد أنَّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم تيمم في المدينة لجواب السلام ؛ فإنّه سلّم عليه رجل وهو يبول ، فلما فرغ تيمم وردّ عليه السلام ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما.
(1) فعن أبي الجهيم رضي الله عنه: أقبل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من نحو بئر جمل فلقيه رجل فلم يرد عليه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حتى أقبل على الجدار فمسح بوجهه ويديه ثم رد عليه السلام» في صحيح مسلم، وصحيح البخاري، وغيرها.
وما ورد من الآثار من جواز التيمم لصلاة الجنازة عند خوف فوتها، أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة والنسائي والطحاوي عن ابن عباس رضي الله عنهما، والطحاوي عن الحسن وإبراهيم وعطاء وابن شهاب وغيرهم، والبيهقي والدارقطني عن ابن عمر رضي الله عنهما.
Or fear of missing the Eid prayer at the beginning): That is, if he fears missing the Eid prayer, he says: Water placed for people to drink – if a man performs wudu with it, that is permissible for him. But if it was placed for people to perform wudu with, it is not permissible for anyone to drink from it. So by analogy to his statement, if he finds water placed for people to drink, it is not permissible for him to perform tayammum. [1] His statement: 'or fear' is a conjunction to the previous excuses. Its summary: If he fears missing the prayer of Eid al-Fitr or Eid al-Adha if he performs wudu, it is permissible for him to perform tayammum and pray the Eid prayer, even if he is healthy and has access to water and is able to use it. That is, if he fears the sun setting and time passing, or the imam finishing the Eid prayer. The reasoning is that the Eid prayer is lost without replacement, for it has no make-up. So the ability to use water is like the inability. If there are multiple Eid prayers in a place and he fears missing it with the imam in a specific mosque, he does not perform tayammum, but rather performs wudu and goes to another mosque. The basis for this is what is reported that the Prophet (peace be upon him) performed tayammum in Medina to respond to a greeting; for a man greeted him while he was urinating, and when he finished, he performed tayammum and returned the greeting – reported by Bukhari and Muslim and others. (1) From Abu al-Juhaym (may Allah be pleased with him): 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) came from the direction of Bi'r Jamal, and a man met him, but the Prophet did not respond to him until he approached a wall, wiped his face and hands, then returned the greeting' – in Sahih Muslim, Sahih Bukhari, etc. And what is reported from the traditions about the permissibility of tayammum for the funeral prayer when fearing missing it – reported by Ibn Abi Shaybah, al-Nasa'i, and al-Tahawi from Ibn 'Abbas (may Allah be pleased with them), and al-Tahawi from al-Hasan, Ibrahim, 'Ata', Ibn Shihab, and others, and al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni from Ibn 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with them).
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It addresses the situation where a person fears missing the Eid prayer if he performs wudu with water. The commentator explains that the water placed for drinking – if used for wudu – is permissible, but if placed for wudu, drinking is prohibited. The core ruling: if one fears missing Eid prayer (due to time or imam finishing), tayammum is allowed even if water is available. The reason is that Eid prayer cannot be made up (fawt la ila khalaf). The ability to use water is disregarded. If there are multiple Eid congregations and he fears missing only a specific imam, he must perform wudu and go to another mosque. The proof includes the hadith of the Prophet performing tayammum to return a greeting after urinating, and athar (traditions) allowing tayammum for funeral prayer due to fear of missing it. The funeral prayer also cannot be made up. This establishes the principle that tayammum is permitted for any act that cannot be repeated (fawt la ila khalaf).

Key Terms
فوت لا إلى خلف (fawt lā ilā khalaf): Loss without replacement: an act that cannot be made up if its time passes, like Eid prayer and funeral prayer.
تيمم (tayammum): Dry ablution with clean earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because Eid prayer is optional
B) Because Eid prayer cannot be made up (fawt la ila khalaf)
C) Because water is always scarce on Eid day
D) Because tayammum is better than wudu

Key Takeaway
Tayammum is permissible for acts that cannot be repeated, like Eid and funeral prayers, even if water is accessible, due to the principle of fawt lā ilā khalaf.

Tayammum for Building on Eid Prayer After Hadath
Intra-Hanafi ruling on tayammum to build on Eid prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the permissibility of tayammum to build on the Eid prayer after hadath, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
Tayammum is allowed to build on Eid prayer when fear of loss remains, even if imam finishes, due to crowding.

This passage addresses a nuanced situation: a person begins Eid prayer with wudu, then hadath occurs. The question is whether tayammum permits them to continue without restarting. The Hanafi school has an internal difference, and understanding this helps clarify the scope of tayammum and the principle of 'fawt la ila khalaf'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the dispute
Read the passage carefully: the person starts Eid prayer with wudu, then hadath occurs. The dispute is about whether tayammum can be used to 'build' (continue) the prayer. Note the conditions: fear of missing the prayer, crowding, and the imam's status.
2
Distinguish the agreed and disputed cases
The passage states three sub-scenarios: (1) if he can catch the imam after wudu, all agree he must do wudu; (2) if he fears the sun setting while doing wudu, all agree tayammum is allowed; (3) the disputed case: no fear of sun setting and no hope of catching the imam. Identify which scenario Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on.
3
Analyze the reasoning of each position
Sahibayn argue that the latecomer can pray after the imam, so there is no fear of loss. Abu Hanifa counters that Eid day is crowded, so praying alone risks disruption, thus fear remains. Consider the principle of 'fawt la ila khalaf' (loss without replacement) and how it applies.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical difference: According to Abu Hanifa, one may perform tayammum and continue the prayer; according to Sahibayn, one must perform wudu and then pray behind the imam, potentially missing the congregation. Also note the Shafi'i position: they do not allow tayammum for Eid at all because it can be made up.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد الشروع متوضئاً والحدث للبناء جاز له أن يتيمم ويشرع فيها، هذا بالاتفاق، وبعد الشروع متوضئاً والحدث للبناء) : أي إذا شرع في صلاة العيد متوضئاً، ثم سبقه الحدث، ويخاف أنه إن توضأ تفوته الصلاة جاز له أن يتيمم للبناء، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة خلافاً لهما، وإن شرع بالتيمم، وسبقه الحدث جاز له التيمم للبناء بالاتفاق. ذلك يدلّ على جواز التيمم لخوف فوت ما يفوت لا إلى خلف، وقد بسطنا كل ذلك في السعاية.
[1] قوله : بالاتفاق ... الخ؛ أي بين أئمتنا الثلاثة خلافاً للشافعي، فإنه لم يجوز التيمم لصلاة العيد ولا صلاة الجنازة، بمجرد خوف الفوت بناءً على أنه يجوز إعادتهما، فلا يتحقق فيهما الفوت لا إلى خلف.
[2] قوله : أي إذا شرع ... الخ؛ توضيحه : أنه إذا شرع في صلاة العيد بالوضوء، وسبقه الحدث في خلال الصلاة، فإن كان لا يخاف ذهاب الوقت ويمكنه أن يدرك شيئاً منها مع الإمام توضأ ولا يتيمم اتفاقاً؛ لإمكان أداء الباقي بعده، وإن خاف زوال الشمس لو اشتغل بالوضوء يباح له التيمم اتفاقاً. ولو لم يخف زوال الشمس ولا رجاء إدراكهما مع الإمام، فعنده يتيمم ويبني خلافاً لهما؛ لأن اللاحق يصلّي بعد فراغ الإمام من صلاته، فلا يخاف الفوت؛ لأنه في حكم الصلاة بالجماعة، وأبو حنيفة نظر إلى أنَّ يوم العيد يوم زحمة، فلعله يعرضُهُ عارض يفسد صلاته إذا صلّى منفرداً فخوف الفوتِ في حقه باق، فلذلك أباح التيمم للبناء. كذا في الهداية، وحواشيها.
[3] قوله : جاز له التيمم؛ لأنا لو أوجبنا عليه الوضوء يكون واجداً للماء في صلاته، فتفسد صلاته، فإنّ المتيمم إذا وجد الماء في خلال صلاته يجب عليه أن يستأنف الصلاة.
And after starting [prayer] in a state of wudu and the hadath [occurs] for building [on the prayer]: It is permissible for him to perform tayammum and begin it (i.e., the prayer) – this is by agreement. And after starting in a state of wudu and the hadath for building: That is, if he starts the Eid prayer in a state of wudu, then the hadath overtakes him, and he fears that if he performs wudu, the prayer will be lost, then it is permissible for him to perform tayammum for building, and this according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (companions). And if he starts with tayammum, and hadath overtakes him, it is permissible for him to perform tayammum for building by agreement. This indicates the permissibility of tayammum due to fear of missing what is lost without replacement, and we have expanded all that in al-Si'ayah. [1] His statement: 'by agreement' etc., meaning between our three imams, contrary to al-Shafi'i, for he did not permit tayammum for the Eid prayer nor the funeral prayer merely due to fear of loss, based on the fact that they can be repeated, so there is no loss without replacement in them. [2] His statement: 'that is, if he starts' etc.; its clarification: If he starts the Eid prayer with wudu, and hadath overtakes him during the prayer, then if he does not fear the passing of time and can catch some of it with the imam, he performs wudu and does not perform tayammum by agreement, because it is possible to perform the remainder after that. And if he fears the sun setting if he busies himself with wudu, then tayammum is permitted for him by agreement. And if he does not fear the sun setting nor hope to catch it with the imam, then according to him (Abu Hanifa) he performs tayammum and builds, contrary to them (the two companions), because the latecomer prays after the imam finishes his prayer, so he does not fear loss; because he is in the ruling of congregational prayer. But Abu Hanifa considered that the day of Eid is a crowded day; perhaps an obstacle will occur that spoils his prayer if he prays alone, so the fear of loss remains for him. For that reason he permitted tayammum for building. Thus in al-Hidayah and its glosses. [3] His statement: 'it is permissible for him to perform tayammum'; because if we make wudu obligatory upon him, he would be one who finds water during his prayer, so his prayer would be invalidated, for if the mutayammim finds water during his prayer, he must restart the prayer.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn) regarding tayammum to build on the Eid prayer after hadath occurs during the prayer. It also presents the Hanafi opposition to Shafi'i. The ruling: If a person starts the Eid prayer with wudu, then hadath occurs, and he fears missing the prayer if he performs wudu, he may perform tayammum and continue (build) the prayer. This is the position of Abu Hanifa, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) disagree. The points of difference: If there is no fear of time passing and he could catch part of the prayer with the imam after wudu, all agree he must perform wudu. If he fears the sun setting, all agree tayammum is allowed. The dispute is when there is no fear of sun setting and no hope of catching the imam (because the imam finishes quickly). Sahibayn say he is like a latecomer who prays after the imam, so there is no fear of losing the prayer—he can do wudu and pray behind the imam. Abu Hanifa argues that Eid day is crowded; if he prays alone, he risks an obstacle invalidating his prayer, so the fear of loss remains. Therefore, tayammum is allowed. The text also clarifies that if he started the prayer with tayammum and then hadath occurs, all agree tayammum is allowed to build. The Shafi'i school does not permit tayammum for Eid or funeral prayer because they consider these prayers repeatable, so the principle of 'fawt la ila khalaf' does not apply. The Hanafi school holds that they are not repeatable, so tayammum is permitted. The justification for tayammum is that requiring wudu would make the person find water during the prayer, which would invalidate the prayer (since a mutayammim who finds water must restart). This demonstrates the principle that tayammum is allowed for any act that is lost without replacement.

Key Terms
بناء (binā'): Building on a prayer: continuing the prayer after a disruption (like hadath) without starting over, by performing tayammum and then continuing from where one left off.
حدث (hadath): A ritual impurity that invalidates wudu, such as passing wind, urine, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why is tayammum allowed to build on Eid prayer even when the imam has finished and there is no fear of time passing?
A) Because the prayer cannot be made up
B) Because the day of Eid is crowded, so praying alone may be disrupted
C) Because tayammum is always better than wudu
D) Because the latecomer must pray after the imam

Key Takeaway
Tayammum for building on Eid prayer is allowed by Abu Hanifa when fear of loss remains, even after the imam finishes, due to the risk of obstacles on a crowded day.
